<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Meditation</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Mental practice of focus on a particular object</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the induction of specific modes or states of consciousness. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Meditation_(disambiguation)" title="Meditation (disambiguation)">Meditation (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Mediation" title="Mediation">mediation</a> or <a href="/wiki/Medication" title="Medication">medication</a>.</div>

title="Buddhist">Buddhist</a> monk <a href="/wiki/Hsuan_Hua" title="Hsuan Hua">Hsuan Hua</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taoist" title="Taoist">Taoist</a> Baduanjin <a href="/wiki/Qigong" title="Qigong">Qigong</a>, the Christian <a href="/wiki/Francis_of_Assisi" title="Francis of Assisi">St Francis</a>, Muslim <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sufis" title="Sufis">Sufis</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dhikr" title="Dhikr">Dhikr</a>, and social reformer <a href="/wiki/Narayana_Guru" title="Narayana Guru">Narayana Guru</a></div></div></div></div>
<p><b>Meditation</b> is a practice in which an individual uses a technique – such as <a href="/wiki/Mindfulness" title="Mindfulness">mindfulness</a>, or focusing the mind on a particular object, thought, or activity – to train <a href="/wiki/Attention" title="Attention">attention</a> and <a href="/wiki/Awareness" title="Awareness">awareness</a>, and achieve a mentally clear and emotionally calm and stable state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWalshShapiro2006228–229_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalshShapiro2006228–229-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECahnPolich2006180_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECahnPolich2006180-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJevningWallaceBeidebach1992415_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJevningWallaceBeidebach1992415-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoleman1988107_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoleman1988107-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MW_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-MW-5">[web 1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford-6">[web 2]</a></sup>
</p><p>Meditation  is practiced in numerous religious traditions. The earliest records of meditation (<i><a href="/wiki/Dhyana_in_Hinduism" title="Dhyana in Hinduism">dhyana</a></i>) are found in the <a href="/wiki/Upanishads" title="Upanishads">Upanishads</a>, and meditation plays a salient role in the contemplative repertoire of <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[5]</a></sup> Since the 19th century, Asian meditative techniques have spread to other cultures where they have also found application in non-spiritual contexts, such as business and health.
</p><p>Meditation may significantly reduce <a href="/wiki/Stress_(biology)" title="Stress (biology)">stress</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anxiety" title="Anxiety">anxiety</a>, <a href="/wiki/Depression_(mood)" title="Depression (mood)">depression</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pain" title="Pain">pain</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[6]</a></sup> and enhance peace, perception,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[7]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Self-concept" title="Self-concept">self-concept</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Well-being" title="Well-being">well-being</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goyal_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-goyal-11">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-12">[10]</a></sup> Research is ongoing to better understand the <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_meditation" title="Effects of meditation">effects of meditation</a> on health (<a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychological</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neurology" title="Neurology">neurological</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardiovascular" title="Cardiovascular">cardiovascular</a>) and other areas.
</p>

</li>
<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-25"><a href="#Hinduism"><span class="tocnumber">4.2.3</span> <span class="toctext">Hinduism</span></a></li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The English <i>meditation</i> is derived from <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">Old French</a> <i>meditacioun</i>, in turn from <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/meditatio" title="wikt:meditatio">meditatio</a></i> from a verb <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/meditor" title="wikt:meditor">meditari</a></i>, meaning "to think, contemplate, devise, ponder".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oel_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-oel-14">[12]</a></sup> In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholic" title="Catholic">Catholic</a> tradition, the use of the term <i>meditatio</i> as part of a formal, stepwise process of meditation goes back to at least the 12th century monk <a href="/wiki/Guigo_II" title="Guigo II">Guigo II</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oel_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-oel-14">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[13]</a></sup> before which the Greek word <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theoria" title="Theoria">Theoria</a> was used for the same purpose.
</p><p>Apart from its historical usage, the term <i>meditation</i> was introduced as a translation for Eastern spiritual practices, referred to as <i>dhyāna</i> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dhyana_in_Hinduism_(Self-knowledge)" title="Dhyana in Hinduism (Self-knowledge)">in Hinduism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> and  which comes from the <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> root <i>dhyai</i>, meaning to contemplate or meditate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-feurstein06_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-feurstein06-17">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[16]</a></sup> The term "meditation" in English may also refer to practices from Islamic <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[17]</a></sup> or other traditions such as Jewish <a href="/wiki/Kabbalah" title="Kabbalah">Kabbalah</a> and Christian <a href="/wiki/Hesychasm" title="Hesychasm">Hesychasm</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoleman1988_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoleman1988-20">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions">Definitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Definitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Difficulties_in_defining_meditation">Difficulties in defining meditation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Difficulties in defining meditation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="No_universally_accepted_definition">No universally accepted definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: No universally accepted definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Meditation has proven difficult to define as it covers a wide range of dissimilar practices in different traditions. In popular usage, the word "meditation" and the phrase "meditative practice" are often used imprecisely to designate practices found across many cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoleman1988_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoleman1988-20">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carroll05_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-carroll05-21">[19]</a></sup> These can include almost anything that is claimed to train the attention of mind or to teach calm or compassion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-22">[20]</a></sup> There remains no definition of necessary and sufficient criteria for meditation that has achieved universal or widespread acceptance within the modern scientific <a href="/wiki/Community" title="Community">community</a>. In 1971, <a href="/wiki/Claudio_Naranjo" title="Claudio Naranjo">Claudio Naranjo</a> noted that "The word 'meditation' has been used to designate a variety of practices that differ enough from one another so that we may find trouble in defining what <i>meditation</i> is."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-naranjo72_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-naranjo72-23">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 6">: 6 </span></sup> A 2009 study noted a "persistent lack of consensus in the literature" and a "seeming intractability of defining meditation".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBondOspinaHootonBialy2009135_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBondOspinaHootonBialy2009135-24">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Separation_of_technique_from_tradition">Separation of technique from tradition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Separation of technique from tradition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some of the difficulty in precisely defining meditation has been in recognizing the particularities of the many various traditions;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-intro_to_consciousness_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-intro_to_consciousness-25">[23]</a></sup> and theories and practice can differ within a tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunne_@_Stanford_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dunne_@_Stanford-26">[24]</a></sup> Taylor noted that even within a faith such as "Hindu" or "Buddhist", schools and individual teachers may teach distinct types of meditation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor99_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-taylor99-27">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 2">: 2 </span></sup>
Ornstein noted that "Most techniques of meditation do not exist as solitary practices but are only artificially separable from an entire system of practice and belief."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ornstein72_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-ornstein72-28">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 143">: 143 </span></sup> For instance, while monks meditate as part of their everyday lives, they also engage the codified rules and live together in monasteries in specific cultural settings that go along with their meditative practices.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dictionary_definitions">Dictionary definitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Dictionary definitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Dictionaries give both the original Latin meaning of "think[ing] deeply about (something)";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford-6">[web 2]</a></sup> as well as the popular usage of "focusing one's mind for a period of time",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford-6">[web 2]</a></sup> "the act of giving your attention to only one thing, either as a religious activity or as a way of becoming calm and relaxed",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[web 3]</a></sup> and "to engage in mental exercise (such as concentrating on one's breathing or repetition of a mantra) for the purpose of reaching a heightened level of spiritual awareness."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MW_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-MW-5">[web 1]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scholarly_definitions">Scholarly definitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Scholarly definitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In modern <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychological</a> research, meditation has been defined and characterized in various ways. Many of these emphasize the role of <a href="/wiki/Attention" title="Attention">attention</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoleman1988_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoleman1988-20">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWalshShapiro2006_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalshShapiro2006-30">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECahnPolich2006_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECahnPolich2006-31">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJevningWallaceBeidebach1992_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJevningWallaceBeidebach1992-32">[29]</a></sup> and characterize the practice of meditation as attempts to get beyond the reflexive, "discursive thinking"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shapiro82discursive_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-shapiro82discursive-34">[note 1]</a></sup> or "logic"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bond09logic_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-bond09logic-35">[note 2]</a></sup> mind<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oknondiscursive_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-oknondiscursive-36">[note 3]</a></sup> to achieve a deeper, more devout, or more relaxed state.
</p><p>Bond et al. (2009) identified criteria for defining a practice as meditation "for use in a comprehensive systematic review of the therapeutic use of meditation", using "a 5-round <a href="/wiki/Delphi_method" title="Delphi method">Delphi study</a> with a panel of 7 experts in meditation research" who were also trained in diverse but empirically highly studied (Eastern-derived or clinical) forms of meditation<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[note 4]</a></sup>:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>three main criteria ... as essential to any meditation practice: the use of a defined technique, logic relaxation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[note 5]</a></sup> and a self-induced state/mode.
</p><p>Other criteria deemed important [but not essential] involve a state of psychophysical relaxation, the use of a self-focus skill or anchor, the presence of a state of suspension of logical thought processes, a religious/spiritual/philosophical context, or a state of mental silence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBondOspinaHootonBialy2009135_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBondOspinaHootonBialy2009135-24">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>
... It is plausible that meditation is best thought of as a natural category of techniques best captured by '<a href="/wiki/Family_resemblance" title="Family resemblance">family resemblances</a>' ... or by the related <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prototype_(linguistics)" title="Prototype (linguistics)">'prototype' model of concepts</a>."<i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBondOspinaHootonBialy2009p._135:_&quot;It_is_plausible_that_meditation_is_best_thought_of_as_a_natural_category_of_techniques_best_captured_by_'[[family_resemblance]]s'_([[Ludwig_Wittgenstein|Wittgenstein]],_1968)_or_by_the_related_[[Prototype_(linguistics)|'prototype'_model_of_concepts]]_([[Eleanor_Rosch|Rosch]],_1973;_Rosch_&amp;_Mervin,_1975).&quot;_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBondOspinaHootonBialy2009p._135:_&quot;It_is_plausible_that_meditation_is_best_thought_of_as_a_natural_category_of_techniques_best_captured_by_'[[family_resemblance]]s'_([[Ludwig_Wittgenstein|Wittgenstein]],_1968)_or_by_the_related_[[Prototype_(linguistics)|'prototype'_model_of_concepts]]_([[Eleanor_Rosch|Rosch]],_1973;_Rosch_&amp;_Mervin,_1975).&quot;-39">[31]</a></sup></i></p></blockquote>
<p>Several other definitions of meditation have been used by influential modern reviews of research on meditation across multiple traditions:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[note 6]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Walsh &amp; Shapiro (2006): "Meditation refers to a family of self-regulation practices that focus on training attention and awareness in order to bring mental processes under greater voluntary control and thereby foster general mental well-being and development and/or specific capacities such as calm, clarity, and concentration"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWalshShapiro2006228–229_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalshShapiro2006228–229-1">[1]</a></sup></li>
<li>Cahn &amp; Polich (2006): "<i>Meditation</i> is used to describe practices that self-regulate the body and mind, thereby affecting mental events by engaging a specific attentional set.... regulation of attention is the central commonality across the many divergent methods"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECahnPolich2006180_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECahnPolich2006180-2">[2]</a></sup></li>
<li>Jevning et al. (1992): "We define meditation... as a stylized mental technique... repetitively practiced for the purpose of attaining a subjective experience that is frequently described as very restful, silent, and of heightened alertness, often characterized as blissful"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJevningWallaceBeidebach1992415_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJevningWallaceBeidebach1992415-3">[3]</a></sup></li>
<li>Goleman (1988): "the need for the meditator to retrain his attention, whether through concentration or mindfulness, is the single invariant ingredient in... every meditation system"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoleman1988107_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoleman1988107-4">[4]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classifications">Classifications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Classifications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Focused_and_open_methods">Focused and open methods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Focused and open methods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In the West, meditation techniques have often been classified in two broad categories, which in actual practice are often combined: focused (or concentrative) meditation and open monitoring (or <a href="/wiki/Mindfulness" title="Mindfulness">mindfulness</a>) meditation:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lutz08_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-lutz08-43">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Direction of mental attention... A practitioner can focus intensively on one particular object (so-called <i>concentrative meditation</i>), on all mental events that enter the field of awareness (so-called <i>mindfulness meditation</i>), or both specific focal points and the field of awareness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBondOspinaHootonBialy2009p._130:_&quot;The_differences_and_similarities_among_these_techniques_is_often_explained_in_the_Western_meditation_literature_in_terms_of_the_direction_of_mental_attention_(Koshikawa_&amp;_Ichii,_1996;_Naranjo,_1971;_Orenstein,_1971):_A_practitioner_can_focus_intensively_on_one_particular_object_(so-called_''concentrative_meditation''),_on_all_mental_events_that_enter_the_field_of_awareness_(so-called_''mindfulness_meditation''),_or_both_specific_focal_points_and_the_field_of_awareness_(Orenstein,_1971).&quot;_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBondOspinaHootonBialy2009p._130:_&quot;The_differences_and_similarities_among_these_techniques_is_often_explained_in_the_Western_meditation_literature_in_terms_of_the_direction_of_mental_attention_(Koshikawa_&amp;_Ichii,_1996;_Naranjo,_1971;_Orenstein,_1971):_A_practitioner_can_focus_intensively_on_one_particular_object_(so-called_''concentrative_meditation''),_on_all_mental_events_that_enter_the_field_of_awareness_(so-called_''mindfulness_meditation''),_or_both_specific_focal_points_and_the_field_of_awareness_(Orenstein,_1971).&quot;-44">[35]</a></sup> </p></blockquote>
<p>Focused methods include <a href="/wiki/Anapanasati" title="Anapanasati">paying attention to the breath</a>, to an idea or feeling (such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mett%C4%81" title="Mettā">mettā (loving-kindness)</a>), to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/K%C5%8Dan" title="Kōan">kōan</a>, or to a <a href="/wiki/Mantra" title="Mantra">mantra</a> (such as in <a href="/wiki/Transcendental_Meditation" title="Transcendental Meditation">transcendental meditation</a>), and single point meditation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[37]</a></sup> Open monitoring methods include <a href="/wiki/Mindfulness" title="Mindfulness">mindfulness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shikantaza" title="Shikantaza">shikantaza</a> and other awareness states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_possible_typologies">Other possible typologies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Other possible typologies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Another typology divides meditation approaches into concentrative, generative, receptive and reflective practices:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGangadharanHemamalini202170_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGangadharanHemamalini202170-48">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAguirre201818-20_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAguirre201818-20-49">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>concentrative: focused attention, including breath meditation, TM, and visualizations;</li>
<li>generative: developing qualities like loving kindness and compassion;</li>
<li>receptive: open monitoring;</li>
<li>reflective: systematic investigation, contemplation.</li></ul>
<p>The Buddhist tradition often divides meditative practice into samatha, or calm abiding,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[42]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vipassana" title="Vipassana">vipassana</a>, insight. <a href="/wiki/Anapanasati" title="Anapanasati">Mindfulness of breathing</a>, a form of focused attention, calms down the mind; this calmed mind can then investigate the nature of reality,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berkeley.edu_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-berkeley.edu-52">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerez-De-AlbenizHolmes2000_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerez-De-AlbenizHolmes2000-54">[45]</a></sup> by monitoring the fleeting and ever-changing constituents of experience, by reflective investigation, or by "turning back the radiance," focusing awareness on awareness itself and discerning the true nature of mind as awareness itself.
</p><p>Matko and Sedlmeier (2019) "call into question the common division into “focused attention” and “open-monitoring” practices." They argue for "two orthogonal dimensions along which meditation techniques could be classified," namely "activation" and "amount of body orientation," proposing seven clusters of techniques: "mindful observation, body-centered meditation, visual concentration, contemplation, affect-centered meditation, mantra meditation, and meditation with movement."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMatkoSedlmeier2019_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMatkoSedlmeier2019-55">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>Jonathan Shear argues that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transcendental_meditation" title="Transcendental meditation">transcendental meditation</a> is an "automatic self-transcending" technique, different from focused attention and open monitoring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Travis,_F._2010_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Travis,_F._2010-56">[47]</a></sup> In this kind of practice, "there is no attempt to sustain any particular condition at all. Practices of this kind, once started, are reported to automatically “transcend” their own activity and disappear, to be started up again later if appropriate."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Travis,_F._2010_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Travis,_F._2010-56">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[note 7]</a></sup> Yet, Shear also states that "automatic self-transcending" also applies to the way other techniques such as from Zen and Qigong are practiced by experienced meditators "once they had become effortless and automatic through years of practice."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Travis,_F._2010_56-3"><a href="#cite_note-Travis,_F._2010-56">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Technique">Technique</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Technique">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Posture">Posture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Posture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1590-MT-au-Peru-2011-Consciousness-Based-Education.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/1590-MT-au-Peru-2011-Consciousness-Based-Education.JPG/220px-1590-MT-au-Peru-2011-Consciousness-Based-Education.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/1590-MT-au-Peru-2011-Consciousness-Based-Education.JPG/330px-1590-MT-au-Peru-2011-Consciousness-Based-Education.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/1590-MT-au-Peru-2011-Consciousness-Based-Education.JPG/440px-1590-MT-au-Peru-2011-Consciousness-Based-Education.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1590-MT-au-Peru-2011-Consciousness-Based-Education.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Young children practicing meditation in a <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peruvian</a> school</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Asana" title="Asana">Asana</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Asana" title="Asana">Asanas</a> and positions such as the <a href="/wiki/Lotus_position" title="Lotus position">full-lotus</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ardha_Padmasana" title="Ardha Padmasana">half-lotus</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cross-legged" title="Cross-legged">Burmese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seiza" title="Seiza">Seiza</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kneeling" title="Kneeling">kneeling</a> positions are popular in Buddhism, Jainism and Hinduism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[48]</a></sup> although other postures such as sitting, supine (lying), and standing are also used. Meditation is also sometimes done while walking, known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kinhin" title="Kinhin">kinhin</a>,  while doing a simple task mindfully, known as <a href="/wiki/Samu_(Zen)" title="Samu (Zen)">samu</a>, or while lying down, known as <a href="/wiki/Shavasana" title="Shavasana">savasana</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Frequency">Frequency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Frequency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Transcendental_Meditation_technique" title="Transcendental Meditation technique">Transcendental Meditation technique</a> recommends practice of 20 minutes twice per day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-huffingtonpost.co.uk_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-huffingtonpost.co.uk-61">[51]</a></sup> Some techniques suggest less time,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berkeley.edu_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-berkeley.edu-52">[43]</a></sup> especially when starting meditation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[52]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Davidson" title="Richard Davidson">Richard Davidson</a> has quoted research saying benefits can be achieved with a practice of only 8 minutes per day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abcnews1_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-abcnews1-63">[53]</a></sup> Research shows improvement in meditation time with simple oral and video training.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[54]</a></sup> Some meditators practice for much longer,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[56]</a></sup> particularly when on a course or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spiritual_retreat" title="Spiritual retreat">retreat</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[57]</a></sup> Some meditators find practice best in <a href="/wiki/Brahmamuhurtha" title="Brahmamuhurtha">the hours before dawn</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Supporting_aids">Supporting aids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Supporting aids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_of_prayer_beads">Use of prayer beads</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Use of prayer beads">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some religions have traditions of using <a href="/wiki/Prayer_beads" title="Prayer beads">prayer beads</a> as tools in devotional meditation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Binz3_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Binz3-69">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sach_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sach-70">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[61]</a></sup> Most prayer beads and Christian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rosary_based_prayers" title="Rosary based prayers">rosaries</a> consist of pearls or beads linked together by a thread.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Binz3_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-Binz3-69">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sach_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sach-70">[60]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Rosary" title="Rosary">Roman Catholic rosary</a> is a string of beads containing five sets with ten small beads. The Hindu <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japa_mala" title="Japa mala">japa mala</a> has 108 beads (the figure 108 in itself having spiritual significance), as well as those used in <a href="/wiki/Gaudiya_Vaishnavism" title="Gaudiya Vaishnavism">Gaudiya Vaishnavism</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Hare_Krishna_(mantra)" title="Hare Krishna (mantra)">Hare Krishna tradition</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhist_prayer_beads" title="Buddhist prayer beads">Buddhist prayer beads</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vishnu_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vishnu-73">[63]</a></sup> Each bead is counted once as a person recites a <a href="/wiki/Mantra" title="Mantra">mantra</a> until the person has gone all the way around the mala.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vishnu_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vishnu-73">[63]</a></sup> The Muslim <a href="/wiki/Misbaha" title="Misbaha">misbaha</a> has 99 beads. There is also quite a variance when it comes to materials used for beads. Beads made from seeds of rudraksha trees are considered sacred by devotees of Shiva, while followers of Vishnu revere the wood that comes from the tulsi plant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Striking_the_meditator">Striking the meditator</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Striking the meditator">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Buddhist literature has many stories of <a href="/wiki/Enlightenment_in_Buddhism" title="Enlightenment in Buddhism">Enlightenment</a> being attained through disciples being struck by their masters. According to T. Griffith Foulk, the <a href="/wiki/Keisaku" title="Keisaku">encouragement stick</a> was an integral part of the Zen practice:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p> In the Rinzai monastery where I trained in the mid-1970s, according to an unspoken etiquette, monks who were sitting earnestly and well were shown respect by being hit vigorously and often; those known as laggards were ignored by the hall monitor or given little taps if they requested to be hit. Nobody asked about the 'meaning' of the stick, nobody explained, and nobody ever complained about its use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[65]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Using_a_narrative">Using a narrative</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Using a narrative">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Neuroscientist and long-time meditator <a href="/wiki/Richard_Davidson" title="Richard Davidson">Richard Davidson</a> has expressed the view that having a narrative can help the maintenance of daily practice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abcnews1_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-abcnews1-63">[53]</a></sup> For instance he himself <a href="/wiki/Prostration_(Buddhism)" title="Prostration (Buddhism)">prostrates</a> to the teachings, and meditates "not primarily for my benefit, but for the benefit of others".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abcnews1_63-2"><a href="#cite_note-abcnews1-63">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Meditation_traditions">Meditation traditions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Meditation traditions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brooklyn_Museum_-_Man_Meditating_in_a_Garden_Setting.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="736" data-file-width="552" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Brooklyn_Museum_-_Man_Meditating_in_a_Garden_Setting.jpg/170px-Brooklyn_Museum_-_Man_Meditating_in_a_Garden_Setting.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Brooklyn_Museum_-_Man_Meditating_in_a_Garden_Setting.jpg/255px-Brooklyn_Museum_-_Man_Meditating_in_a_Garden_Setting.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Brooklyn_Museum_-_Man_Meditating_in_a_Garden_Setting.jpg/340px-Brooklyn_Museum_-_Man_Meditating_in_a_Garden_Setting.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brooklyn_Museum_-_Man_Meditating_in_a_Garden_Setting.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Man Meditating in a Garden Setting</i></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The history of meditation is intimately bound up with the religious context within which it was practiced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEverlyLating2002199–202_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEverlyLating2002199–202-76">[66]</a></sup> Rossano has suggested that the emergence of the capacity for focused attention, an element of many methods of meditation, may have contributed to the latest phases of human biological evolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[67]</a></sup> Some of the earliest references to meditation, as well as proto-Samkhya, are found in the <a href="/wiki/Upanishads" title="Upanishads">Upanishads</a> of <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELusthaus2018_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELusthaus2018-79">[69]</a></sup> The earliest clear references to meditation are in the middle <a href="/wiki/Upanishads" title="Upanishads">Upanishads</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mahabharata" title="Mahabharata">Mahabharata</a> (including the <a href="/wiki/Bhagavad_Gita" title="Bhagavad Gita">Bhagavad Gita</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[71]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Gavin_Flood" title="Gavin Flood">Gavin Flood</a>, the earlier <a href="/wiki/Brihadaranyaka_Upanishad" title="Brihadaranyaka Upanishad">Brihadaranyaka Upanishad</a> is describing meditation when it states that "having become calm and concentrated, one perceives the self (<i>ātman</i>) within oneself" (BU 4.4.23).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flood_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Flood-82">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indian_religions">Indian religions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Indian religions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Jainism">Jainism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Jainism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Jain_meditation" title="Jain meditation">Jain meditation</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kevalajnana.jpg"><img alt="Painting of Mahavira meditating under a tree" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="422" data-file-width="464" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Kevalajnana.jpg/220px-Kevalajnana.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Kevalajnana.jpg/330px-Kevalajnana.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Kevalajnana.jpg/440px-Kevalajnana.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kevalajnana.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i>āsana</i> in which Mahavira is said to have attained omniscience</div></div></div>
<p>Jain meditation and spiritual practices system were referred to as salvation-path. It has three parts called the <i><a href="/wiki/Ratnatraya" title="Ratnatraya">Ratnatraya</a></i> "Three Jewels": right perception and faith, right knowledge and right conduct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ratna_traya_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ratna_traya-83">[73]</a></sup> Meditation in Jainism aims at realizing the self, attaining salvation, and taking the soul to complete freedom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blessings_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-blessings-84">[74]</a></sup> It aims to reach and to remain in the pure state of soul which is believed to be pure consciousness, beyond any attachment or aversion. The practitioner strives to be just a knower-seer (Gyata-Drashta). Jain meditation can be broadly categorized to <i>Dharmya Dhyana</i> and <i>Shukla Dhyana</i>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (June 2019)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Jainism uses meditation techniques such as <i>pindāstha-dhyāna, padāstha-dhyāna, rūpāstha-dhyāna, rūpātita-dhyāna, and savīrya-dhyāna</i>. In <i>padāstha dhyāna</i> one focuses on a <a href="/wiki/Mantra" title="Mantra">mantra</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HN4U_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-HN4U-85">[75]</a></sup> A mantra could be either a combination of core letters or words on deity or themes. There is a rich tradition of Mantra in Jainism. All Jain followers irrespective of their sect, whether <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digambar" title="Digambar">Digambara</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Svetambara" title="Svetambara">Svetambara</a>, practice mantra. Mantra chanting is an important part of daily lives of Jain monks and followers. Mantra chanting can be done either loudly or silently in mind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HN4U_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-HN4U-85">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>Contemplation is a very old and important meditation technique. The practitioner meditates deeply on subtle facts. In <i>agnya vichāya</i>, one contemplates on seven facts – life and non-life, the inflow, bondage, stoppage and removal of <i>karmas</i>, and the final accomplishment of liberation. In <i>apaya vichāya</i>, one contemplates on the incorrect insights one indulges, which eventually develops right insight. In <i>vipaka vichāya</i>, one reflects on the eight causes or basic types of <i>karma</i>. In <i>sansathan vichāya</i>, one thinks about the vastness of the universe and the loneliness of the soul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HN4U_85-2"><a href="#cite_note-HN4U-85">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Buddhism">Buddhism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Buddhism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_meditation" title="Buddhist meditation">Buddhist meditation</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BodhidharmaYoshitoshi1887.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1148" data-file-width="796" decoding="async" height="245" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/BodhidharmaYoshitoshi1887.jpg/170px-BodhidharmaYoshitoshi1887.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/BodhidharmaYoshitoshi1887.jpg/255px-BodhidharmaYoshitoshi1887.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/BodhidharmaYoshitoshi1887.jpg/340px-BodhidharmaYoshitoshi1887.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BodhidharmaYoshitoshi1887.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bodhidharma" title="Bodhidharma">Bodhidharma</a> practicing <a href="/wiki/Zazen" title="Zazen">zazen</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhists</a> pursue meditation as part of the path toward <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bodhi" title="Bodhi">awakening</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nirvana" title="Nirvana">nirvana</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[note 8]</a></sup> The closest words for meditation in the classical languages of Buddhism are <i><a href="/wiki/Bhavana" title="Bhavana">bhāvanā</a></i> ("development"), and the core practices of body contemplations (<a href="/wiki/Patikulamanasikara" title="Patikulamanasikara">repulsiveness</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mara%E1%B9%87asati" title="Maraṇasati">cemetery contemplations</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Anapanasati" title="Anapanasati">anapanasati</a> (mindfulness of in-&amp;-out breathing)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[note 9]</a></sup> culminating in <i>jhāna</i>/<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dhy%C4%81na_in_Buddhism" title="Dhyāna in Buddhism">dhyāna</a></i> or <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sam%C4%81dhi" title="Samādhi">samādhi</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[note 10]</a></sup>
</p><p>While most classical and contemporary Buddhist meditation guides are school-specific,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[note 11]</a></sup> the root meditative practices of various body recollections and <a href="/wiki/Anapanasati" title="Anapanasati">breath meditation</a> have been preserved and transmitted in almost all <a href="/wiki/Schools_of_Buddhism" title="Schools of Buddhism">Buddhist traditions</a>, through <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_texts" title="Buddhist texts">Buddhist texts</a> like the <a href="/wiki/Satipatthana_Sutta" title="Satipatthana Sutta">Satipatthana Sutta</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dhyana_sutras" title="Dhyana sutras">Dhyana sutras</a>, and through oral teacher-student transmissions. These ancient practices are supplemented with various distinct interpretations of, and developments in, these practices. 
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Therav%C4%81da" title="Theravāda">Theravāda</a> tradition stresses the development of samatha and vipassana, postulating over fifty methods for developing mindfulness based on the <a href="/wiki/Satipatthana_Sutta" title="Satipatthana Sutta">Satipatthana Sutta</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[note 12]</a></sup> and forty for developing concentration based on the <a href="/wiki/Visuddhimagga" title="Visuddhimagga">Visuddhimagga</a>. 
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_Buddhism" title="Tibetan Buddhism">Tibetan tradition</a> incorporated <a href="/wiki/Sarvastivada" title="Sarvastivada">Sarvastivada</a> and Tantric practices, wedded with <a href="/wiki/Madhyamaka" title="Madhyamaka">Madhyamaka</a> philosophy, and developed thousands of visualization meditations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[note 13]</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="No_thought"></span>Via the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dhyana_sutras" title="Dhyana sutras">Dhyana sutras</a>, which are based on the Sarvastivada-tradition, the Zen-tradition incorporated mindfulness and breath-meditation. Downplaying the "petty complexities" of satipatthana and the body-recollections<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharf2015475_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharf2015475-93">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcRae1986116_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcRae1986116-94">[78]</a></sup> (but maintaing the awareness of immanent death), the early Chan-tradition developed the notions or practices of <i>wu nian</i> ("no thought, no "fixation on thought, such as one's own views, experiences, and knowledge")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYu2021157_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYu2021157-95">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELai2008351_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELai2008351-96">[80]</a></sup> and <i>fēi sīliàng</i> (非思量, Japanese: <i>hishiryō</i>, "nonthinking");<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESuzuki2014112_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESuzuki2014112-97">[81]</a></sup> and <i>kanxin</i> ("observing the mind")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchaik201870,_93_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaik201870,_93-98">[82]</a></sup> and <i>shou-i pu i</i> (守一不移, "maintaining the one without wavering,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcRae1986143_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcRae1986143-99">[83]</a></sup> turning the attention from the objects of experience, to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nature_of_mind" title="Nature of mind">nature of mind</a>, the perceiving subject itself, which is equated with <a href="/wiki/Buddha-nature" title="Buddha-nature">Buddha-nature</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharf2014939_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharf2014939-100">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road_transmission_of_Buddhism" title="Silk Road transmission of Buddhism">Silk Road transmission of Buddhism</a> introduced meditation to other Asian countries, reaching China in the 2nd century CE,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[85]</a></sup> and Japan in the 6th century CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[86]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Modern_era" title="Modern era">modern era</a>, Buddhist meditation techniques have become popular in the wider world, due to the influence of <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_modernism" title="Buddhist modernism">Buddhist modernism</a> on Asian Buddhism, and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_the_West" title="Buddhism in the West">western lay interest</a> in <a href="/wiki/Zen" title="Zen">Zen</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Vipassana_movement" title="Vipassana movement">Vipassana movement</a>, with many non-Buddhists taking-up meditative practices. The modernized concept of mindfulness (based on the Buddhist term <i><a href="/wiki/Sati_(Buddhism)" title="Sati (Buddhism)">sati</a></i>) and related meditative practices have in turn led to <a href="/wiki/Mindfulness#Therapy_programs" title="Mindfulness">mindfulness based therapies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Dhyana">Dhyana</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Dhyana">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dhy%C4%81na_in_Buddhism" title="Dhyāna in Buddhism">Dhyana</a> may have been an original contribution of <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha" title="Gautama Buddha">Gautama Buddha</a> (5th cent. BCE), the founder of Buddhism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993-104">[88]</a></sup> While often presented as a form of focused attention or concentration, as in Buddhagosa's Theravada classic the <a href="/wiki/Visuddhimagga" title="Visuddhimagga">Visuddhimagga</a> ("Path of purification, 5th c. CE), according to a number contemporary scholars and scholar-practitioners, it is actually a description of the development of perfected equanimity and mindfulness, apparently induced by satipatthana, an open monitoring of the breath, without trying to regulate it. The same description, in a different formula, can be found in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bojjhanga" title="Bojjhanga">bojjhanga</a>, the "seven factors of awakening," and may therefor refer to the core program of early Buddhist <i>bhavana</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[89]</a></sup> According to Vetter, dhyana seems to be a natural development from the sense-restraint and moral constrictions prescribed by the Buddhist tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Samatha_and_vipassana">Samatha and vipassana</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Samatha and vipassana">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>The Buddha identified two paramount mental qualities that arise from wholesome meditative practice or <i>bhavana</i>, namely <i><a href="/wiki/Samatha" title="Samatha">samatha</a></i> ("calm," "serenity"  "tranquility") and <i>vipassana</i> (insight). As the developing tradition started to emphasize the value of liberating insight, and <i>dhyana</i> came to be understood as concentration,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993131_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993131-108">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVetter1988xxi–xxxvii_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVetter1988xxi–xxxvii-109">[93]</a></sup> <i>samatha</i> and <i>vipassana</i> were understood as two distinct meditative techniques. In this understanding, <i>samatha</i> steadies, composes, unifies and concentrates the mind, while <i>vipassana</i> enables one to see, explore and discern "formations" (conditioned phenomena based on the five <a href="/wiki/Skandha" title="Skandha">aggregates</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[note 14]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to this understanding, which is central to Theravada orthodoxy but also plays a role in Tibetan Buddhism, through the meditative development of serenity, one is able to weaken the obscuring <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five_Hindrances" title="Five Hindrances">hindrances</a> and bring the mind to a collected, pliant, and still state (<a href="/wiki/Samadhi" title="Samadhi">samadhi</a>). This quality of mind then supports the development of insight and wisdom (<a href="/wiki/Praj%C3%B1%C4%81_(Buddhism)" title="Prajñā (Buddhism)">Prajñā</a>) which is the quality of mind that can "clearly see" (<i>vi-passana</i>) the nature of phenomena. What exactly is to be seen varies within the Buddhist traditions. In Theravada, all phenomena are to be seen as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anicca" title="Anicca">impermanent</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dukkha" title="Dukkha">suffering</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatta" title="Anatta">not-self</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shunyata" title="Shunyata">empty</a>. When this happens, one develops dispassion (<i>viraga</i>) for all phenomena, including all negative qualities and hindrances and lets them go. It is through the release of the hindrances and ending of craving through the meditative development of insight that one gains liberation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hinduism">Hinduism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Hinduism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu_meditation" title="Hindu meditation">Hindu meditation</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Yoga" title="Yoga">Yoga</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Patanjali_Statue.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="540" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Patanjali_Statue.jpg/220px-Patanjali_Statue.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Patanjali_Statue.jpg/330px-Patanjali_Statue.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Patanjali_Statue.jpg/440px-Patanjali_Statue.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Patanjali_Statue.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A statue of <a href="/wiki/Patanjali" title="Patanjali">Patañjali</a> practicing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dhyana_in_Hinduism_(Self-knowledge)" title="Dhyana in Hinduism (Self-knowledge)">dhyana</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Padmaasana" title="Padmaasana">Padma-asana</a> at <a href="/wiki/Patanjali_Yogpeeth" title="Patanjali Yogpeeth">Patanjali Yogpeeth</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>There are many schools and styles of meditation within <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flood_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-Flood-82">[72]</a></sup> In pre-modern and traditional Hinduism, <i>Yoga</i> and <i>Dhyana</i> are practised to recognize 'pure awareness', or 'pure consciousness', undisturbed by the workings of the mind, as one's eternal self. In <a href="/wiki/Advaita_Vedanta" title="Advaita Vedanta">Advaita Vedanta</a> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jivatman" title="Jivatman">jivatman</a></i>, individual self, is recognized as illusory, and in Reality identical with the omnipresent and <a href="/wiki/Nondualism" title="Nondualism">non-dual</a> <a href="/wiki/%C4%80tman_(Hinduism)" title="Ātman (Hinduism)">Ātman</a>-<a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">Brahman</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/Yoga_(philosophy)" title="Yoga (philosophy)">dualistic Yoga school</a> and <a href="/wiki/Samkhya" title="Samkhya">Samkhya</a>, the Self is called <a href="/wiki/Purusha" title="Purusha">Purusha</a>, a pure consciousness undisturbed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prakriti" title="Prakriti">Prakriti</a>, 'nature'. Depending on the tradition, the liberative event is named <a href="/wiki/Moksha" title="Moksha">moksha</a>, vimukti or <a href="/wiki/Kaivalya" title="Kaivalya">kaivalya</a>.
</p><p>One of the most influential texts of classical Hindu Yoga is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pata%C3%B1jali" title="Patañjali">Patañjali</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Yoga_Sutras_of_Patanjali" title="Yoga Sutras of Patanjali">Yoga sutras</a> (c. 400 CE), a text associated with Yoga and Samkhya, which outlines <a href="/wiki/Ashtanga_(eight_limbs_of_yoga)" title="Ashtanga (eight limbs of yoga)">eight limbs</a> leading to <a href="/wiki/Kaivalya" title="Kaivalya">kaivalya</a> ("aloneness"). These are ethical discipline (<a href="/wiki/Yamas" title="Yamas">yamas</a>), rules (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Niyamas" title="Niyamas">niyamas</a>), physical postures (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C4%80sanas" title="Āsanas">āsanas</a>), breath control (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pr%C4%81%E1%B9%87%C4%81yama" title="Prāṇāyama">prāṇāyama</a>), withdrawal from the senses (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Praty%C4%81h%C4%81ra" title="Pratyāhāra">pratyāhāra</a>), one-pointedness of mind (<a href="/wiki/Dh%C4%81ra%E1%B9%87%C4%81" title="Dhāraṇā">dhāraṇā</a>), meditation (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dhy%C4%81na_in_Hinduism" title="Dhyāna in Hinduism">dhyāna</a>), and finally <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sam%C4%81dhi" title="Samādhi">samādhi</a>.
</p><p>Later developments in Hindu meditation include the compilation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hatha_Yoga" title="Hatha Yoga">Hatha Yoga</a> (forceful yoga) compendiums like the <a href="/wiki/Hatha_Yoga_Pradipika" title="Hatha Yoga Pradipika">Hatha Yoga Pradipika</a>, the development of <a href="/wiki/Bhakti_yoga" title="Bhakti yoga">Bhakti yoga</a> as a major form of meditation, and <a href="/wiki/Tantra" title="Tantra">Tantra</a>. Another important Hindu yoga text is the <a href="/wiki/Yoga_Yajnavalkya" title="Yoga Yajnavalkya">Yoga Yajnavalkya</a>, which makes use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hatha_Yoga" title="Hatha Yoga">Hatha Yoga</a> and Vedanta Philosophy.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sikhism">Sikhism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Sikhism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/N%C4%81m_Jap%C5%8D" title="Nām Japō">Nām Japō</a></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Sikhism" title="Sikhism">Sikhism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simran_(Sanskrit_word)" title="Simran (Sanskrit word)">simran</a> (meditation) and good deeds are both necessary to achieve the devotee's Spiritual goals;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[95]</a></sup> without good deeds meditation is futile. When Sikhs meditate, they aim to feel <a href="/wiki/Akal_(Sikh_term)" title="Akal (Sikh term)">God</a>'s presence and emerge in the divine light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[96]</a></sup> It is only God's <a href="/wiki/Hukam" title="Hukam">divine will</a> or order that allows a devotee to desire to begin to meditate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[97]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/N%C4%81m_Jap%C5%8D" title="Nām Japō">Nām Japnā</a> involves focusing one's attention on the names or great attributes of God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="East_Asian_religions_-_Taoism">East Asian religions - Taoism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: East Asian religions - Taoism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daoist_meditation" title="Daoist meditation">Daoist meditation</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stage1.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="756" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Stage1.gif/170px-Stage1.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Stage1.gif/255px-Stage1.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Stage1.gif/340px-Stage1.gif 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stage1.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Gathering the Light", Taoist meditation from <i><a href="/wiki/The_Secret_of_the_Golden_Flower" title="The Secret of the Golden Flower">The Secret of the Golden Flower</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>Taoist meditation has developed techniques including concentration, visualization, <i><a href="/wiki/Qi" title="Qi">qi</a></i> cultivation, contemplation, and mindfulness meditations in its long history. Traditional Daoist meditative practices were influenced by <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Buddhism" title="Chinese Buddhism">Chinese Buddhism</a> from around the 5th century and influenced <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_medicine" title="Traditional Chinese medicine">Traditional Chinese medicine</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_martial_arts" title="Chinese martial arts">Chinese martial arts</a>.
</p><p>Livia Kohn distinguishes three basic types of Taoist meditation: "concentrative", "insight", and "visualization".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[99]</a></sup> <i>Ding</i> <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%AE%9A" title="wikt:定">定</a> (literally means "decide; settle; stabilize") refers to "deep concentration", "intent contemplation", or "perfect absorption". <i>Guan</i> <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E8%A7%80" title="wikt:觀">觀</a> (lit. "watch; observe; view") meditation seeks to merge and attain unity with the Dao. It was developed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tang_Dynasty" title="Tang Dynasty">Tang Dynasty</a> (618–907) Taoist masters based upon the <i><a href="/wiki/Tiantai" title="Tiantai">Tiantai</a></i> Buddhist practice of <i><a href="/wiki/Vipassan%C4%81" title="Vipassanā">Vipassanā</a></i> "insight" or "wisdom" meditation. <i>Cun</i> <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%AD%98" title="wikt:存">存</a> (lit. "exist; be present; survive") has a sense of "to cause to exist; to make present" in the meditation techniques popularized by the Taoist <a href="/wiki/Shangqing_School" title="Shangqing School">Shangqing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lingbao_School" title="Lingbao School">Lingbao Schools</a>. A meditator visualizes or actualizes solar and lunar essences, lights, and deities within their body, which supposedly results in health and longevity, even <i><a href="/wiki/Xian_(Taoism)" title="Xian (Taoism)">xian</a></i> 仙/仚/僊, "immortality".
</p><p>The (late 4th century BCE) <i><a href="/wiki/Guanzi_(text)" title="Guanzi (text)">Guanzi</a></i> essay <i><a href="/wiki/Neiye" title="Neiye">Neiye</a></i> "Inward training" is the oldest received writing on the subject of <i><a href="/wiki/Qi" title="Qi">qi</a></i> cultivation and breath-control meditation techniques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cambridge_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge-117">[100]</a></sup> For instance, "When you enlarge your mind and let go of it, when you relax your vital breath and expand it, when your body is calm and unmoving: And you can maintain the One and discard the myriad disturbances. ... This is called "revolving the vital breath": Your thoughts and deeds seem heavenly."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>The (c. 3rd century BCE) Taoist <i><a href="/wiki/Zhuangzi_(book)" title="Zhuangzi (book)">Zhuangzi</a></i> records <i><a href="/wiki/Zuowang" title="Zuowang">zuowang</a></i> or "sitting forgetting" meditation. <a href="/wiki/Confucius" title="Confucius">Confucius</a> asked his disciple <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yan_Hui_(disciple_of_Confucius)" title="Yan Hui (disciple of Confucius)">Yan Hui</a> to explain what "sit and forget" means: "I slough off my limbs and trunk, dim my intelligence, depart from my form, leave knowledge behind, and become identical with the Transformational Thoroughfare."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>Taoist meditation practices are central to <a href="/wiki/Chinese_martial_arts" title="Chinese martial arts">Chinese martial arts</a> (and some <a href="/wiki/Japanese_martial_arts" title="Japanese martial arts">Japanese martial arts</a>), especially the <i>qi</i>-related <i><a href="/wiki/Neijia" title="Neijia">neijia</a></i> "internal martial arts". Some well-known examples are <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tao_Yin" title="Tao Yin">daoyin</a></i> "guiding and pulling", <i><a href="/wiki/Qigong" title="Qigong">qigong</a></i> "life-energy exercises", <i><a href="/wiki/Neigong" title="Neigong">neigong</a></i> "internal exercises", <i><a href="/wiki/Neidan" title="Neidan">neidan</a></i> "internal alchemy", and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taijiquan" title="Taijiquan">taijiquan</a></i> "great ultimate boxing", which is thought of as moving meditation. One common explanation contrasts "movement in stillness" referring to energetic visualization of <i>qi</i> circulation in <i>qigong</i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zuochan" title="Zuochan">zuochan</a></i> "seated meditation",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerez-De-AlbenizHolmes2000_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerez-De-AlbenizHolmes2000-54">[45]</a></sup> versus "stillness in movement" referring to a state of meditative calm in <i>taijiquan</i> forms. Also the unification or middle road forms such as Wuxingheqidao that seeks the unification of internal alchemical forms with more external forms.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Monotheistic_religions">Monotheistic religions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Monotheistic religions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Judaism">Judaism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Judaism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Jewish_meditation" title="Jewish meditation">Jewish meditation</a></div>
<p>Judaism has made use of meditative practices for thousands of years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Verman1_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Verman1-120">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[104]</a></sup> For instance, in the <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">Torah</a>, the patriarch <a href="/wiki/Isaac" title="Isaac">Isaac</a> is described as going <i>"לשוח"</i> (<i>lasuach</i>) in the field – a term understood by all commentators as some type of meditative practice (<a href="/wiki/Book_of_Genesis" title="Book of Genesis">Genesis</a> 24:63).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaplan1978101_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaplan1978101-122">[105]</a></sup> Similarly, there are indications throughout the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tanakh" title="Tanakh">Tanakh</a> (the Hebrew <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a>) that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Testament_prophets" title="Old Testament prophets">prophets</a> meditated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Verman45_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Verman45-123">[106]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a>, there are two <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a> words for meditation: <i>hāgâ</i> (<a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="he">הגה</span>), <i>to sigh</i> or <i>murmur</i>, but also <i>to meditate</i>, and <i>sîḥâ</i> (<a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="he">שיחה</span>),  <i>to muse</i>, or <i>rehearse in one's mind</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kaplan,_A._1985_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kaplan,_A._1985-124">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>Classical Jewish texts espouse a wide range of meditative practices, often associated with the cultivation of <i><a href="/wiki/Kavanah" title="Kavanah">kavanah</a></i> or intention. The first layer of <a href="/wiki/Halakha" title="Halakha">rabbinic law</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Mishnah" title="Mishnah">Mishnah</a>, describes ancient sages "waiting" for an hour before their prayers, "in order to direct their hearts to the Omnipresent One (<a href="/wiki/Mishnah" title="Mishnah">Mishnah</a> <a href="/wiki/Berakhot_(tractate)" title="Berakhot (tractate)">Berakhot</a> 5:1). Other early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rabbinic_texts" title="Rabbinic texts">rabbinic texts</a> include instructions for visualizing the Divine Presence (B. <a href="/wiki/Talmud" title="Talmud">Talmud</a> <a href="/wiki/Sanhedrin_(tractate)" title="Sanhedrin (tractate)">Sanhedrin</a> 22a) and breathing with conscious gratitude for every breath (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genesis_Rabba" title="Genesis Rabba">Genesis Rabba</a> 14:9).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the best-known types of meditation in early Jewish mysticism was the work of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Merkabah" title="Merkabah">Merkabah</a>, from the root /R-K-B/ meaning "chariot" (of God).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kaplan,_A._1985_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kaplan,_A._1985-124">[107]</a></sup> Some meditative traditions have been encouraged in <a href="/wiki/Kabbalah" title="Kabbalah">Kabbalah</a>, and some Jews have described Kabbalah as an inherently meditative field of study.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaplan1982_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaplan1982-127">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[111]</a></sup> Kabbalistic meditation often involves the mental visualization of the supernal realms. <a href="/wiki/Aryeh_Kaplan" title="Aryeh Kaplan">Aryeh Kaplan</a> has argued that the ultimate purpose of Kabbalistic meditation is to understand and cleave to the Divine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kaplan,_A._1985_124-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kaplan,_A._1985-124">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>Meditation has been of interest to a wide variety of modern Jews. In modern Jewish practice, one of the best known meditative practices is called <i>"<a href="/wiki/Hitbodedut" title="Hitbodedut">hitbodedut</a>"</i> (<i>התבודדות</i>, alternatively transliterated as "hisbodedus"), and is explained in <a href="/wiki/Kabbalah" title="Kabbalah">Kabbalistic</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hasidic" title="Hasidic">Hasidic</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mussar_Movement" title="Mussar Movement">Mussar</a> writings, especially the Hasidic method of Rabbi <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nachman_of_Breslav" title="Nachman of Breslav">Nachman of Breslav</a>. The word derives from the Hebrew word "boded" (בודד), meaning the state of being alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaplan1978op_cit_p._2_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaplan1978op_cit_p._2-129">[112]</a></sup> Another Hasidic system is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habad" title="Habad">Habad</a> method of "hisbonenus", related to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sephirah" title="Sephirah">Sephirah</a> of "Binah", Hebrew for understanding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaplan1982op_cit,_p._13_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaplan1982op_cit,_p._13-130">[113]</a></sup> This practice is the analytical reflective process of making oneself understand a mystical concept well, that follows and internalises its study in Hasidic writings. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Musar_Movement" title="Musar Movement">Musar Movement</a>, founded by Rabbi Israel Salanter in the middle of the nineteenth-century, emphasized meditative practices of <a href="/wiki/Introspection" title="Introspection">introspection</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mental_image" title="Mental image">visualization</a> that could help to improve moral character.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[114]</a></sup> Conservative rabbi <a href="/wiki/Alan_Lew" title="Alan Lew">Alan Lew</a> has emphasized meditation playing an important role in the process of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teshuvah" title="Teshuvah"><i>teshuvah</i> (repentance)</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[116]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jewish_Buddhist" title="Jewish Buddhist">Jewish Buddhists</a> have adopted Buddhist styles of meditation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[117]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Christianity">Christianity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Christianity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
