<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Padre_Pio.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="736" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="244" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Padre_Pio.jpg/170px-Padre_Pio.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Padre_Pio.jpg/255px-Padre_Pio.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Padre_Pio.jpg/340px-Padre_Pio.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Padre_Pio.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Saint <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pio_of_Pietrelcina" title="Pio of Pietrelcina">Pio of Pietrelcina</a> stated: "Through the study of books one seeks God; by meditation one finds Him."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[118]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Christian_meditation" title="Christian meditation">Christian meditation</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Christian_meditation" title="Christian meditation">Christian meditation</a> is a term for a form of prayer in which a structured attempt is made to get in touch with and deliberately reflect upon the revelations of <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">God</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[119]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>, by 20 BCE <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philo_of_Alexandria" title="Philo of Alexandria">Philo of Alexandria</a> had written on some form of "spiritual exercises" involving attention (prosoche) and concentration<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[120]</a></sup> and by the 3rd century <a href="/wiki/Plotinus" title="Plotinus">Plotinus</a> had developed meditative techniques. The word meditation comes from the Latin word <i>meditatum</i>, which means to "concentrate" or "to ponder". Monk <a href="/wiki/Guigo_II" title="Guigo II">Guigo II</a> introduced this terminology for the first time in the 12th century AD. Christian meditation is the process of deliberately focusing on specific thoughts (e.g. a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biblical" title="Biblical">biblical</a> scene involving <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virgin_Mary" title="Virgin Mary">Virgin Mary</a>) and reflecting on their meaning in the context of the love of God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[121]</a></sup> Christian meditation is sometimes taken to mean the middle level in a broad three-stage characterization of prayer: it then involves more reflection than first level vocal <a href="/wiki/Prayer" title="Prayer">prayer</a>, but is more structured than the multiple layers of <a href="/wiki/Contemplation" title="Contemplation">contemplation</a> in Christianity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p>Between the 10th and 14th centuries, <a href="/wiki/Hesychasm" title="Hesychasm">hesychasm</a> was developed, particularly on <a href="/wiki/Mount_Athos" title="Mount Athos">Mount Athos</a> in Greece, and involves the repetition of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesus_prayer" title="Jesus prayer">Jesus prayer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[123]</a></sup> Interactions with Indians or the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sufis" title="Sufis">Sufis</a> may have influenced the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Christian" title="Eastern Christian">Eastern Christian</a> meditation approach to hesychasm, but this is unproven.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Christian" title="Western Christian">Western Christian</a> meditation contrasts with most other approaches in that it does not involve the repetition of any phrase or action and requires no specific posture. Western Christian meditation progressed from the 6th century practice of Bible reading among <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Order_of_Saint_Benedict" title="Order of Saint Benedict">Benedictine</a> monks called <a href="/wiki/Lectio_Divina" title="Lectio Divina">Lectio Divina</a>, i.e. divine reading. Its four formal steps as a "ladder" were defined by the monk <a href="/wiki/Guigo_II" title="Guigo II">Guigo II</a> in the 12th century with the Latin terms <i>lectio</i>, <i>meditatio</i>, <i>oratio</i>, and <i>contemplatio</i> (i.e. read, ponder, pray, contemplate). Western Christian meditation was further developed by saints such as <a href="/wiki/Ignatius_of_Loyola" title="Ignatius of Loyola">Ignatius of Loyola</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teresa_of_Avila" title="Teresa of Avila">Teresa of Avila</a> in the 16th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Christianity</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Rosary" title="Rosary">Rosary</a> is a devotion for the meditation of the mysteries of Jesus and Mary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[131]</a></sup> “The gentle repetition of its prayers makes it an excellent means to moving into deeper meditation. It gives us an opportunity to open ourselves to God’s word, to refine our interior gaze by turning our minds to the life of Christ. The first principle is that meditation is learned through practice. Many people who practice rosary meditation begin very simply and gradually develop a more sophisticated meditation. The meditator learns to hear an interior voice, the voice of God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[132]</a></sup> Similarly, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chotki" title="Chotki">chotki</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox" title="Eastern Orthodox">Eastern Orthodox</a> denomination, the <a href="/wiki/Wreath_of_Christ" title="Wreath of Christ">Wreath of Christ</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lutheran" title="Lutheran">Lutheran</a> faith, and the <a href="/wiki/Anglican_prayer_beads" title="Anglican prayer beads">Anglican prayer beads</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglican" title="Anglican">Episcopalian</a> tradition are used for Christian prayer and meditation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dhiman2020_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dhiman2020-150">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Winston2008_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Winston2008-151">[134]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edmund_P._Clowney" title="Edmund P. Clowney">Edmund P. Clowney</a>, Christian meditation contrasts with Eastern forms of meditation as radically as the portrayal of <a href="/wiki/God_the_Father" title="God the Father">God the Father</a> in the Bible contrasts with depictions of <a href="/wiki/Krishna" title="Krishna">Krishna</a> or <a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">Brahman</a> in Indian teachings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[135]</a></sup> Unlike some Eastern styles, most styles of Christian meditation do not rely on the repeated use of <a href="/wiki/Mantra" title="Mantra">mantras</a>, and yet are also intended to stimulate thought and deepen meaning. Christian meditation aims to heighten the personal relationship based on the love of God that marks Christian communion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[137]</a></sup> In <i><a href="/wiki/Aspects_of_Christian_meditation" title="Aspects of Christian meditation">Aspects of Christian meditation</a></i>, the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> warned of potential incompatibilities in mixing Christian and Eastern styles of meditation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[138]</a></sup> In 2003, in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A_Christian_reflection_on_the_New_Age" title="A Christian reflection on the New Age">A Christian reflection on the New Age</a></i> the <a href="/wiki/Holy_See" title="Holy See">Vatican</a> announced that the "Church avoids any concept that is close to those of the <a href="/wiki/New_Age" title="New Age">New Age</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[141]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Islam">Islam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Islam">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Muraqabah" title="Muraqabah">Muraqabah</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sama_(Sufism)" title="Sama (Sufism)">Sama (Sufism)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dhikr#Sufi_view" title="Dhikr">Dhikr § Sufi view</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Whriling_dervishes,_Rumi_Fest_2007.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="889" decoding="async" height="253" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Whriling_dervishes%2C_Rumi_Fest_2007.jpg/220px-Whriling_dervishes%2C_Rumi_Fest_2007.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Whriling_dervishes%2C_Rumi_Fest_2007.jpg/330px-Whriling_dervishes%2C_Rumi_Fest_2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Whriling_dervishes%2C_Rumi_Fest_2007.jpg/440px-Whriling_dervishes%2C_Rumi_Fest_2007.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Whriling_dervishes,_Rumi_Fest_2007.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Whirling dervishes</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Salah" title="Salah">Salah</a> is a mandatory act of devotion performed by Muslims five times per day. The body goes through sets of different postures, as the mind attains a level of concentration called <i>khushu</i>.
</p><p>A second optional type of meditation, called <a href="/wiki/Dhikr" title="Dhikr">dhikr</a>, meaning remembering and mentioning God, involved the repetition of the 99 Names of God since the 8th or 9th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prayer_page_147-149_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-Prayer_page_147-149-159">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Education'_page_63_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-Education'_page_63-160">[143]</a></sup> It is interpreted in different meditative techniques in <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufism</a> or Islamic mysticism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prayer_page_147-149_159-1"><a href="#cite_note-Prayer_page_147-149-159">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Education'_page_63_160-1"><a href="#cite_note-Education'_page_63-160">[143]</a></sup> This became one of the essential elements of Sufism as it was systematized traditionally. It is juxtaposed with <i>fikr</i> (thinking) which leads to knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[144]</a></sup> By the 12th century, the practice of Sufism included specific meditative techniques, and its followers practiced breathing controls and the repetition of holy words.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spiritual_Psychology'_page_109_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spiritual_Psychology'_page_109-162">[145]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufism</a> uses a meditative procedure like Buddhist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samadhi_(Buddhism)" title="Samadhi (Buddhism)">concentration</a>, involving high-intensity and sharply focused <a href="/wiki/Introspection" title="Introspection">introspection</a>. In the Oveyssi-Shahmaghsoudi Sufi order, for example, <a href="/wiki/Muraqabah" title="Muraqabah">muraqabah</a> takes the form of tamarkoz, "concentration" in <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Tafakkur</i> or <i>tadabbur</i> in Sufism literally means <i>reflection upon the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a></i>: this is considered to permit access to a form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cognitive" title="Cognitive">cognitive</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emotion" title="Emotion">emotional</a> development that can emanate only from the higher level, i.e. from God. The sensation of receiving <a href="/wiki/Revelation" title="Revelation">divine inspiration</a> awakens and liberates both <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">heart</a> and <a href="/wiki/Intellect" title="Intellect">intellect</a>, permitting such inner growth that the apparently mundane actually takes on the quality of the <a href="/wiki/Infinity" title="Infinity">infinite</a>. Muslim teachings embrace life as a test of one's submission to God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[147]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Dervish" title="Dervish">Dervishes</a> of certain Sufi orders practice <a href="/wiki/Sufi_whirling" title="Sufi whirling">whirling</a>, a form of physically active meditation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[148]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Bah.C3.A1.CA.BC.C3.AD_Faith"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Baháʼí_Faith">Baháʼí Faith</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Baháʼí Faith">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In the teachings of the <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_Faith" title="Baháʼí Faith">Baháʼí Faith</a>, meditation is a primary tool for spiritual development,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bahai-meditation_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-bahai-meditation-166">[149]</a></sup> involving reflection on the words of God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith-meditation_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-meditation-167">[150]</a></sup> While <a href="/wiki/Prayer" title="Prayer">prayer</a> and meditation are linked, where meditation happens generally in a prayerful attitude, prayer is seen specifically as turning toward God,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith-prayer_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-prayer-168">[151]</a></sup> and meditation is seen as a communion with one's self where one focuses on the divine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith-meditation_167-1"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-meditation-167">[150]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_teachings" title="Baháʼí teachings">Baháʼí teachings</a> the purpose of meditation is to strengthen one's understanding of the words of God, and to make one's soul more susceptible to their potentially transformative power,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith-meditation_167-2"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-meditation-167">[150]</a></sup> more receptive to the need for both prayer and meditation to bring about and maintain a spiritual communion with God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[152]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%27u%27ll%C3%A1h" title="Bahá'u'lláh">Bahá'u'lláh</a>, the founder of the religion, never specified any particular form of meditation, and thus each person is free to choose their own form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bahai-meditation_166-1"><a href="#cite_note-bahai-meditation-166">[149]</a></sup> However, he did state that Baháʼís should read a passage of the <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_literature" title="Baháʼí literature">Baháʼí writings</a> twice a day, once in the morning, and once in the evening, and meditate on it. He also encouraged people to reflect on one's actions and worth at the end of each day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith-meditation_167-3"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-meditation-167">[150]</a></sup> During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nineteen_Day_Fast" title="Nineteen Day Fast">Nineteen Day Fast</a>, a period of the year during which Baháʼís adhere to a sunrise-to-sunset <a href="/wiki/Fasting" title="Fasting">fast</a>, they meditate and pray to reinvigorate their spiritual forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-directives_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-directives-170">[153]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_spirituality">Modern spirituality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Modern spirituality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jawlensky_Meditation_1290065.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4233" data-file-width="3209" decoding="async" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Jawlensky_Meditation_1290065.jpg/170px-Jawlensky_Meditation_1290065.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Jawlensky_Meditation_1290065.jpg/255px-Jawlensky_Meditation_1290065.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Jawlensky_Meditation_1290065.jpg/340px-Jawlensky_Meditation_1290065.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jawlensky_Meditation_1290065.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Meditation</i>. <a href="/wiki/Alexej_von_Jawlensky" title="Alexej von Jawlensky">Alexej von Jawlensky</a>, 1918</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_dissemination_in_the_West">Modern dissemination in the West</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Modern dissemination in the West">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Meditation has spread in the West since the late 19th century, accompanying increased travel and communication among cultures worldwide. Most prominent has been the transmission of Asian-derived practices to the West. In addition, interest in some Western-based meditative practices has been revived,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reininger98_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-reininger98-171">[154]</a></sup> and these have been disseminated to a limited extent to Asian countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-contempout_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-contempout-172">[155]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ideas about Eastern meditation had begun "seeping into American popular culture even before the American Revolution through the various sects of European occult Christianity",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor99_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-taylor99-27">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 3">: 3 </span></sup> and such ideas "came pouring in [to America] during the era of the transcendentalists, especially between the 1840s and the 1880s."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor99_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-taylor99-27">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 3">: 3 </span></sup> The following decades saw further spread of these ideas to America:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Parliament_of_Religions" title="World Parliament of Religions">World Parliament of Religions</a>, held in Chicago in 1893, was the landmark event that increased Western awareness of meditation. This was the first time that Western audiences on American soil received Asian spiritual teachings from Asians themselves. Thereafter, <a href="/wiki/Swami_Vivekananda" title="Swami Vivekananda">Swami Vivekananda</a>... [founded] various <a href="/wiki/Vedanta" title="Vedanta">Vedanta</a> ashrams... <a href="/wiki/Anagarika_Dharmapala" title="Anagarika Dharmapala">Anagarika Dharmapala</a> lectured at Harvard on Theravada Buddhist meditation in 1904; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abdul_Baha" title="Abdul Baha">Abdul Baha</a> ... [toured] the US teaching the principles of <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_Faith" title="Baháʼí Faith">Bahai</a>  [<i><a href="/wiki/Sic" title="Sic">sic</a></i>], and <a href="/wiki/Soyen_Shaku" title="Soyen Shaku">Soyen Shaku</a> toured in 1907 teaching Zen...<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor99_27-3"><a href="#cite_note-taylor99-27">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 4">: 4 </span></sup></p></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Meditating_in_Madison_Square_Park.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1540" data-file-width="2512" decoding="async" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Meditating_in_Madison_Square_Park.jpg/220px-Meditating_in_Madison_Square_Park.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Meditating_in_Madison_Square_Park.jpg/330px-Meditating_in_Madison_Square_Park.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Meditating_in_Madison_Square_Park.jpg/440px-Meditating_in_Madison_Square_Park.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Meditating_in_Madison_Square_Park.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Meditating in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Madison_Square_Park" title="Madison Square Park">Madison Square Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a></div></div></div>
<p>More recently, in the 1960s, another surge in Western interest in meditative practices began. The rise of communist political power in Asia led to many Asian spiritual teachers taking refuge in Western countries, oftentimes as refugees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor99_27-4"><a href="#cite_note-taylor99-27">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 7">: 7 </span></sup> In addition to spiritual forms of meditation, secular forms of meditation have taken root. Rather than focusing on spiritual growth, secular meditation emphasizes stress reduction, relaxation and self-improvement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEverlyLating2002200_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEverlyLating2002200-173">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[157]</a></sup>
</p><p>The 2012 US National Health Interview Survey (NHIS) (34,525 subjects) found 8% of US adults used meditation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[158]</a></sup> with lifetime and 12-month prevalence of meditation use of 5.2% and 4.1% respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[159]</a></sup> In the 2017 NHIS survey, meditation use among workers was 10% (up from 8% in 2002).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[160]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mantra meditation, with the use of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japa_mala" title="Japa mala">japa mala</a> and especially with focus on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hare_Krishna_maha-mantra" title="Hare Krishna maha-mantra">Hare Krishna maha-mantra</a>, is a central practice of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaudiya_Vaishnava" title="Gaudiya Vaishnava">Gaudiya Vaishnava</a> faith tradition and the <a href="/wiki/International_Society_for_Krishna_Consciousness" title="International Society for Krishna Consciousness">International Society for Krishna Consciousness</a> (ISKCON), also known as the Hare Krishna movement. Other popular <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Religious_Movements" title="New Religious Movements">New Religious Movements</a> include the <a href="/wiki/Ramakrishna_Mission" title="Ramakrishna Mission">Ramakrishna Mission</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vedanta_Society" title="Vedanta Society">Vedanta Society</a>, <a href="/wiki/Divine_Light_Mission" title="Divine Light Mission">Divine Light Mission</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chinmaya_Mission" title="Chinmaya Mission">Chinmaya Mission</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rajneesh" title="Rajneesh">Osho</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sahaja_Yoga" title="Sahaja Yoga">Sahaja Yoga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transcendental_Meditation" title="Transcendental Meditation">Transcendental Meditation</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kalki_Bhagavan" title="Kalki Bhagavan">Oneness University</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brahma_Kumaris" title="Brahma Kumaris">Brahma Kumaris</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vihangam_Yoga" title="Vihangam Yoga">Vihangam Yoga</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heartfulness_Meditation" title="Heartfulness Meditation">Heartfulness Meditation (Sahaj Marg)</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Age">New Age</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: New Age">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/New_Age" title="New Age">New Age</a> meditations are often influenced by Eastern philosophy, mysticism, <a href="/wiki/Yoga" title="Yoga">yoga</a>, Hinduism and Buddhism, yet may contain some degree of Western influence. In the West, meditation found its mainstream roots through the <a href="/wiki/Counterculture_of_the_1960s" title="Counterculture of the 1960s">social revolution of the 1960s and 1970s</a>, when many of the <a href="/wiki/Baby_boomers" title="Baby boomers">youth of the day</a> rebelled against traditional religion as a reaction against what some perceived as the failure of Christianity to provide spiritual and ethical guidance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[161]</a></sup>
New Age meditation as practised by the early hippies is regarded for its techniques of blanking out the mind and releasing oneself from conscious thinking. This is often aided by repetitive chanting of a mantra, or focusing on an object.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[162]</a></sup> New Age meditation evolved into a range of purposes and practices, from serenity and balance to access to other realms of consciousness to the concentration of energy in group meditation to the supreme goal of <a href="/wiki/Samadhi" title="Samadhi">samadhi</a>, as in the ancient yogic practice of meditation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[163]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Guided_meditation">Guided meditation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Guided meditation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Yoga_nidra" title="Yoga nidra">Yoga nidra</a></div>
<p>Guided meditation is a form of meditation which utilizes a number of different techniques to achieve or enhance the meditative state. It may simply be meditation done under the guidance of a trained practitioner or teacher, or it may be through the use of imagery, music, and other techniques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[164]</a></sup> The session can be either in person, via <a href="/wiki/Media_(communication)" title="Media (communication)">media</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[165]</a></sup> comprising <a href="/wiki/Meditation_music" title="Meditation music">music</a> or verbal instruction, or a combination of both.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[166]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[167]</a></sup> The most common form is a combination of <a href="/wiki/Meditation_music" title="Meditation music">meditation music</a> and <a href="/wiki/Music_therapy" title="Music therapy">receptive music therapy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guided_imagery" title="Guided imagery">guided imagery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Relaxation_technique" title="Relaxation technique">relaxation</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mindfulness_(psychology)" title="Mindfulness (psychology)">mindfulness</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Writing_therapy" title="Writing therapy">journaling</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[170]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because of the different combinations used under the one term, it can be difficult to attribute positive or negative outcomes to any of the various techniques. Furthermore, the term is frequently used interchangeably with "guided imagery" and sometimes with "creative visualization" in <a href="/wiki/Popular_psychology" title="Popular psychology">popular psychology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Self-help" title="Self-help">self-help</a> literature. It is less commonly used in <a href="/wiki/Scholarly_method" title="Scholarly method">scholarly</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific</a> publications. Consequently, guided meditation cannot be understood as a single technique but rather multiple techniques that are integral to its practice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[172]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[174]</a></sup>
</p><p>Guided meditation as an aggregate or synthesis of techniques includes <a href="/wiki/Meditation_music" title="Meditation music">meditation music</a>, <a href="/wiki/Music_therapy" title="Music therapy">receptive music therapy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guided_imagery" title="Guided imagery">guided imagery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Relaxation_technique" title="Relaxation technique">relaxation</a>, meditative praxis, and <a href="/wiki/Writing_therapy" title="Writing therapy">self-reflective diary-keeping or journaling.</a> All of which have been shown to have <a href="/wiki/Therapy" title="Therapy">therapeutic</a> benefits when employed as an adjunct to primary strategies. Benefits include lower levels of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stress_(psychological)" title="Stress (psychological)">stress</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[175]</a></sup> reducing <a href="/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">asthmatic episodes</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[176]</a></sup> physical <a href="/wiki/Pain_management" title="Pain management">pain</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[177]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Insomnia" title="Insomnia">insomnia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[178]</a></sup> episodic <a href="/wiki/Anger" title="Anger">anger</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[179]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_distortion" title="Cognitive distortion">negative or irrational thinking</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[180]</a></sup> 
and <a href="/wiki/Anxiety" title="Anxiety">anxiety</a>, as well as improving <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coping_(psychology)" title="Coping (psychology)">coping skills</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[181]</a></sup> focus,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[182]</a></sup> 
and a general feeling of <a href="/wiki/Well-being" title="Well-being">well-being</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[183]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[184]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Secular_applications">Secular applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Secular applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Psychotherapy">Psychotherapy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Psychotherapy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Analytic_psychology" title="Analytic psychology">Analytic psychology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Psychoanalysis" title="Psychoanalysis">Psychoanalysis</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Carl_Jung" title="Carl Jung">Carl Jung</a> (1875–1961) was an early western explorer of eastern religious practices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[185]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[186]</a></sup> He clearly advocated ways to increase the conscious awareness of an individual. Yet he expressed some caution concerning a westerner's direct immersion in eastern practices without some prior appreciation of the differing spiritual and cultural contexts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[187]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[188]</a></sup> Also <a href="/wiki/Erich_Fromm" title="Erich Fromm">Erich Fromm</a> (1900–1980) later explored spiritual practices of the east.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[189]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Clinical_applications">Clinical applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Clinical applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Mindfulness#Applications" title="Mindfulness">Mindfulness applications</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mindfulness-based_stress_reduction" title="Mindfulness-based stress reduction">Mindfulness-based stress reduction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mindfulness-based_cognitive_therapy" title="Mindfulness-based cognitive therapy">Mindfulness-based cognitive therapy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mindfulness-based_pain_management" title="Mindfulness-based pain management">Mindfulness-based pain management</a></div>
<p>The US <a href="/wiki/National_Center_for_Complementary_and_Integrative_Health" title="National Center for Complementary and Integrative Health">National Center for Complementary and Integrative Health</a> states that <i>"Meditation is a mind and body practice that has a long history of use for increasing calmness and physical relaxation, improving psychological balance, coping with illness, and enhancing overall health and well-being."</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nih_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-nih-207">[190]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-12">[10]</a></sup> A 2014 review found that practice of mindfulness meditation for two to six months by people undergoing long-term <a href="/wiki/Psychiatry" title="Psychiatry">psychiatric</a> or medical therapy could produce small improvements in <a href="/wiki/Anxiety" title="Anxiety">anxiety</a>, pain, or <a href="/wiki/Depression_(mood)" title="Depression (mood)">depression</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goyal_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goyal-208">[191]</a></sup> In 2017, the <a href="/wiki/American_Heart_Association" title="American Heart Association">American Heart Association</a> issued a scientific statement that meditation may be a reasonable <a href="/wiki/Adjuvant_therapy" title="Adjuvant therapy">adjunct</a> practice to help reduce the risk of <a href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_disease" title="Cardiovascular disease">cardiovascular diseases</a>, with the qualification that meditation needs to be better defined in higher-quality <a href="/wiki/Clinical_research" title="Clinical research">clinical research</a> of these disorders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levine_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-Levine-209">[192]</a></sup> Recent findings have also found evidence of meditation effecting migraines in adults. Mindfulness meditation may allow for a decrease in migraine episodes, and a drop in migraine medication usage.<b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[193]</a></sup></b>
</p><p>Low-quality evidence indicates that meditation may help with <a href="/wiki/Irritable_bowel_syndrome" title="Irritable bowel syndrome">irritable bowel syndrome</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nih_207-1"><a href="#cite_note-nih-207">[190]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Insomnia" title="Insomnia">insomnia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nih_207-2"><a href="#cite_note-nih-207">[190]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dementia" title="Dementia">cognitive decline</a> in the elderly,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gard_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gard-211">[194]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Post-traumatic_stress_disorder" title="Post-traumatic stress disorder">post-traumatic stress disorder</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gallegos_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gallegos-212">[195]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bisson_Roberts_Andrew_Cooper_p._213-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bisson_Roberts_Andrew_Cooper_p.-213">[196]</a></sup> Researchers have found that participating in mindfulness meditation can aid insomnia patients by improving sleep quality and total wake time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:03_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-:03-214">[197]</a></sup> Mindfulness meditation is not a treatment for insomnia patients, but it can provide support in addition to their treatment options.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:03_214-1"><a href="#cite_note-:03-214">[197]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Meditation_in_the_workplace">Meditation in the workplace</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Meditation in the workplace">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A 2010 review of the literature on <a href="/wiki/Spirituality" title="Spirituality">spirituality</a> and performance in organizations found an increase in corporate meditation programs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-215">[198]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2016 around a quarter of U.S. employers were using stress reduction initiatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-216">[199]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-217">[200]</a></sup> The goal was to help reduce stress and improve reactions to stress. Aetna now offers its program to its customers. <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a> also implements mindfulness, offering more than a dozen meditation courses, with the most prominent one, "Search Inside Yourself", having been implemented since 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_217-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-217">[200]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/General_Mills" title="General Mills">General Mills</a> offers the Mindful Leadership Program Series, a course which uses a combination of mindfulness meditation, yoga and dialogue with the intention of developing the mind's capacity to pay attention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_217-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-217">[200]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relaxation_response_and_biofeedback">Relaxation response and biofeedback</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Relaxation response and biofeedback">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Herbert_Benson" title="Herbert Benson">Herbert Benson</a> of <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Medical_School" title="Harvard Medical School">Harvard Medical School</a> conducted a series of clinical tests on meditators from various disciplines, including the <a href="/wiki/Transcendental_Meditation_technique" title="Transcendental Meditation technique">Transcendental Meditation technique</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_Buddhism" title="Tibetan Buddhism">Tibetan Buddhism</a>. In 1975, Benson published a book titled <i><a href="/wiki/The_Relaxation_Response" title="The Relaxation Response">The Relaxation Response</a></i> where he outlined his own version of meditation for relaxation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[201]</a></sup> Also in the 1970s, the American psychologist Patricia Carrington developed a similar technique called Clinically Standardized Meditation (CSM).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[202]</a></sup> In Norway, another sound-based method called <a href="/wiki/Acem_Meditation" title="Acem Meditation">Acem Meditation</a> developed a psychology of meditation and has been the subject of several scientific studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[203]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Biofeedback" title="Biofeedback">Biofeedback</a> has been used by many researchers since the 1950s in an effort to enter deeper states of mind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[204]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[205]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects">Effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_meditation" title="Effects of meditation">Effects of meditation</a></div>
<p>Research on the processes and effects of meditation is a subfield of <a href="/wiki/Neurology" title="Neurology">neurological</a> research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goyal_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-goyal-11">[9]</a></sup> Modern scientific techniques, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FMRI" title="FMRI">fMRI</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EEG" title="EEG">EEG</a>, were used to observe neurological responses during meditation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[206]</a></sup>  Concerns have been raised on the quality of meditation research,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goyal_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-goyal-11">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VanDam_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-VanDam-224">[207]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stetka_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-stetka-225">[208]</a></sup> including the particular characteristics of individuals who tend to participate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[209]</a></sup>
</p><p>Meditation lowers heart rate, oxygen consumption, breathing frequency, <a href="/wiki/Stress_hormone" title="Stress hormone">stress hormones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lactic_acid" title="Lactic acid">lactate</a> levels, and <a href="/wiki/Sympathetic_nervous_system" title="Sympathetic nervous system">sympathetic nervous system</a> activity (associated with the <a href="/wiki/Fight-or-flight_response" title="Fight-or-flight response">fight-or-flight response</a>), along with a modest decline in blood pressure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[210]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[211]</a></sup> However, those who have meditated for two or three years were found to already have low blood pressure. During meditation, the oxygen consumption decrease averages 10 to 20 percent over the first three minutes. During sleep for example, oxygen consumption decreases around 8 percent over four or five hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[212]</a></sup> For meditators who have practiced for years, breath rate can drop to three or four breaths per minute and brain waves slow from <a href="/wiki/Alpha_wave" title="Alpha wave">alpha waves</a> seen in normal relaxation to much slower <a href="/wiki/Delta_wave" title="Delta wave">delta</a> and <a href="/wiki/Theta_wave" title="Theta wave">theta waves</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[213]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since the 1970s, <a href="/wiki/Clinical_psychology" title="Clinical psychology">clinical psychology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Psychiatry" title="Psychiatry">psychiatry</a> have developed meditation techniques for numerous psychological conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harrington2015_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harrington2015-231">[214]</a></sup> Mindfulness practice is employed in psychology to alleviate mental and physical conditions, such as reducing <a href="/wiki/Depression_(mood)" title="Depression (mood)">depression</a>, stress, and <a href="/wiki/Anxiety" title="Anxiety">anxiety</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goyal_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-goyal-11">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strauss_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-Strauss-232">[215]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Khoury2015_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-Khoury2015-233">[216]</a></sup> Mindfulness is also used in the treatment of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drug_addiction" title="Drug addiction">drug addiction</a>, although the quality of research has been poor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stetka_225-1"><a href="#cite_note-stetka-225">[208]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chiesa_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chiesa-234">[217]</a></sup> Studies demonstrate that meditation has a moderate effect to reduce pain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goyal_11-4"><a href="#cite_note-goyal-11">[9]</a></sup> There is insufficient evidence for any effect of meditation on positive mood, attention, eating habits, sleep, or body weight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goyal_11-5"><a href="#cite_note-goyal-11">[9]</a></sup> A 2015 study, including subjective and objective reports and brain scans, has shown that meditation can improve controlling attention, as well as self-awareness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[218]</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2017 systematic review and meta-analysis of the effects of meditation on <a href="/wiki/Empathy" title="Empathy">empathy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Compassion" title="Compassion">compassion</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prosocial" title="Prosocial">prosocial</a> behaviors found that meditation practices had small to medium effects on self-reported and observable outcomes, concluding that such practices can "improve positive prosocial emotions and behaviors".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[219]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources_(medicine)" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources (medicine)"><span title="Material near this tag may rely on an unreliable or less reliable medical source. I think the article is at high risk of biases: one of the main reasons is that most of the studies considered lack a placebo group (August 2020)">unreliable medical source?</span></a></i>]</sup> However, a meta-review published on <i><a href="/wiki/Scientific_Reports" title="Scientific Reports">Scientific Reports</a></i> showed that the evidence is very weak and "that the effects of meditation on compassion were only significant when compared to passive control groups suggests that other forms of active interventions (like watching a nature video) might produce similar outcomes to meditation".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[220]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Potential_adverse_effects">Potential adverse effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Potential adverse effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_meditation#Potential_adverse_effects_and_limits_of_meditation" title="Effects of meditation">Effects of meditation § Potential adverse effects and limits of meditation</a></div>
<p>Meditation has been correlated with unpleasant experiences in some people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[221]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[222]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[223]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[224]</a></sup>
In some cases, it has also been linked to psychosis in a few individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[225]</a></sup>
</p><p>In one study, published in 2019, of 1,232 regular meditators with at least two months of meditation experience, about a quarter reported having had particularly unpleasant meditation-related experiences (such as  anxiety, fear, distorted emotions or thoughts, altered sense of self or the world), which they thought may have been caused by their meditation practice. Meditators with high levels of repetitive negative thinking and those who only engage in deconstructive meditation were more likely to report unpleasant side effects. Adverse effects were less frequently reported in women and religious meditators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plos_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-plos-243">[226]</a></sup>
</p><p>Difficult experiences encountered in meditation are mentioned in traditional sources; and some may be considered to be just an expected part of the process: for example: seven stages of purification mentioned in Theravāda Buddhism, or possible “unwholesome or frightening visions” mentioned in a practical manual on vipassanā meditation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[227]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meditation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-columns references-column-width" style="column-width: 35em;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-shapiro82discursive-34"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-shapiro82discursive_34-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">An influential definition by <a href="#CITEREFShapiro1982">Shapiro (1982)</a> states that "<i>meditation refers to a family of techniques which have in common a conscious attempt to focus attention in a nonanalytical way and an attempt not to dwell on discursive, ruminating thought</i>" (p. 6, italics in original). The term "discursive thought" has long been used in Western philosophy, and is often viewed as a synonym to logical thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[30]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-bond09logic-35"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-bond09logic_35-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFBondOspinaHootonBialy2009">Bond et al. (2009)</a> report that 7 expert scholars who had studied different traditions of meditation agreed that an "essential" component of meditation "Involves logic relaxation: not 'to intend' to analyze the possible psychophysical effects, not 'to intend' to judge the possible results, not 'to intend' to create any type of expectation regarding the process" (p. 134, Table 4). In their final consideration, all 7 experts regarded this feature as an "essential" component of meditation; none of them regarded it as merely "important but not essential" (p. 234, Table 4). (This same result is presented in Table B1 in <a href="#CITEREFOspinaBondKarkhanehTjosvold2007">Ospina et al. 2007</a>, p. 281)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-oknondiscursive-36"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-oknondiscursive_36-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This does not mean that all meditation seeks to take a person beyond <i>all</i> thought processes, only those processes that are sometimes referred to as "discursive" or "logical" (see <a href="#CITEREFShapiro1982">Shapiro 1982</a>/1984; <a href="#CITEREFBondOspinaHootonBialy2009">Bond et al. 2009</a>; Appendix B, pp. 279–82 in <a href="#CITEREFOspinaBondKarkhanehTjosvold2007">Ospina et al. (2007)</a>).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-37">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"members were chosen on the basis of their publication record of research on the therapeutic use of meditation, their knowledge of and training in traditional or clinically developed meditation techniques, and their affiliation with universities and research centers. Each member had specific expertise and training in at least one of the following meditation practices: <a href="/wiki/Kundalini_yoga" title="Kundalini yoga">kundalini yoga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transcendental_Meditation" title="Transcendental Meditation">Transcendental Meditation</a>, relaxation response, <a href="/wiki/Mindfulness-based_stress_reduction" title="Mindfulness-based stress reduction">mindfulness-based stress reduction</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vipassana" title="Vipassana">vipassana</a> meditation" (<a href="#CITEREFBondOspinaHootonBialy2009">Bond et al. 2009</a>, p. 131); their views were combined using "the <a href="/wiki/Delphi_method" title="Delphi method">Delphi technique</a> ... a method of eliciting and refining group judgments to address complex problems with a high level of uncertainty" (<a href="#CITEREFBondOspinaHootonBialy2009">Bond et al. 2009</a>, p. 131).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-38"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-38">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFBondOspinaHootonBialy2009">Bond et al. 2009</a>: "Logic relaxation is defined by the authors as “not ‘to intend’ to analyzing (not trying to explain) the possible psychophysical effects,” “not ‘to intend’ to judging (good, bad, right, wrong) the possible psychophysical [effects],” and “not ‘to intend’ to creating any type of expectation regarding the process. (Cardoso et al., 2004, p. 59)"</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-42"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-42">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Regarding influential reviews encompassing <i>multiple</i> methods of meditation: <a href="#CITEREFWalshShapiro2006">Walsh &amp; Shapiro (2006)</a>, <a href="#CITEREFCahnPolich2006">Cahn &amp; Polich (2006)</a>, and <a href="#CITEREFJevningWallaceBeidebach1992">Jevning, Wallace &amp; Beidebach (1992)</a>, are cited &gt;80 times in <a href="/wiki/PsycINFO" title="PsycINFO">PsycINFO</a>. Number of citations in <a href="/wiki/PsycINFO" title="PsycINFO">PsycINFO</a>: 254 for Walsh &amp; Shapiro, 2006 (26 August 2018); 561 for Cahn &amp; Polich, 2006 (26 August 2018); 83 for Jevning et al. (1992) (26 August 2018). Goleman's <a href="/wiki/The_Varieties_of_the_Meditative_Experience" title="The Varieties of the Meditative Experience">book</a> has 33 editions listed in WorldCat: 17 editions as <i>The meditative mind: The varieties of meditative experience</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[32]</a></sup> and 16 editions as <i>The varieties of meditative experience</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[33]</a></sup> 
Citation and edition counts are as of August 2018 and September 2018 respectively.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-57"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-57">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to Shear, "Focused Attention, Open Monitoring and Automatic Self-Transcending were likely to be associated with (γ and β)13, θ, and α1 EEG bands, respectively."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Travis,_F._2010_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-Travis,_F._2010-56">[47]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-87"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-87">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For instance, <a href="#CITEREFKamalashila2003">Kamalashila (2003</a>, p. 4)<span class="error harv-error" style="display: none; font-size:100%"> harvtxt error: no target: CITEREFKamalashila2003 (<a href="/wiki/Category:Harv_and_Sfn_template_errors" title="Category:Harv and Sfn template errors">help</a>)</span>, states that Buddhist meditation "includes any method of meditation that has <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bodhi" title="Bodhi">Enlightenment</a> as its <i>ultimate</i> aim." Likewise, <a href="#CITEREFBodhi1999">Bodhi (1999)</a><span class="error harv-error" style="display: none; font-size:100%"> harvtxt error: no target: CITEREFBodhi1999 (<a href="/wiki/Category:Harv_and_Sfn_template_errors" title="Category:Harv and Sfn template errors">help</a>)</span> writes: "To arrive at the experiential realization of the truths it is necessary to take up the practice of meditation.... At the climax of such contemplation the mental eye ... shifts its focus to the unconditioned state, <a href="/wiki/Nirvana" title="Nirvana">Nibbana</a>...." A similar although in some ways slightly broader definition is provided by:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFischer-SchreiberEhrhardDiener1991142_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer-SchreiberEhrhardDiener1991142-86">[76]</a></sup> "<b>Meditation</b> – general term for a multitude of religious practices, often quite different in method, but all having the same goal: to bring the consciousness of the practitioner to a state in which he can come to an experience of 'awakening,' 'liberation,' 'enlightenment.'" <a href="#CITEREFKamalashila2003">Kamalashila (2003)</a><span class="error harv-error" style="display: none; font-size:100%"> harvtxt error: no target: CITEREFKamalashila2003 (<a href="/wiki/Category:Harv_and_Sfn_template_errors" title="Category:Harv and Sfn template errors">help</a>)</span> further allows that some Buddhist meditations are "of a more preparatory nature" (p. 4).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-88"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-88">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/P%C4%81li" title="Pāli">Pāli</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> word <i>bhāvanā</i> literally means "development" as in "mental development." For the association of this term with "meditation," see <a href="#CITEREFEpstein1995">Epstein (1995</a>, p. 105); and <a href="#CITEREFFischer-SchreiberEhrhardDiener1991">Fischer-Schreiber, Ehrhard &amp; Diener (1991</a>, p. 20). As an example from a well-known discourse of the <a href="/wiki/Pali_Canon" title="Pali Canon">Pali Canon</a>, in "The Greater Exhortation to Rahula" (<i>Maha-Rahulovada Sutta</i>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Majjhima_Nikaya" title="Majjhima Nikaya">MN</a> 62), Ven. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sariputta" title="Sariputta">Sariputta</a> tells Ven. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rahula" title="Rahula">Rahula</a> (in Pali, based on <a class="external text" href="http://www.tipitaka.org/romn/cscd/s0202m.mul1.xml" rel="nofollow">VRI, n.d.)</a>: <i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">ānāpānassatiṃ, rāhula, bhāvanaṃ bhāvehi.</i></span></i> <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1067248974">.mw-parser-output cite.citation{font-style:inherit;word-wrap:break-word}.mw-parser-output .citation q{quotes:"\"""\"""'""'"}.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-left{padding-left:0.2em}.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-right{padding-right:0.2em}.mw-parser-output .citation .mw-selflink{font-weight:inherit}</style><cite class="citation web cs1" id="CITEREFṬhānissaro_Bhikkhu2006"><a href="/wiki/%E1%B9%ACh%C4%81nissaro_Bhikkhu" title="Ṭhānissaro Bhikkhu">Ṭhānissaro Bhikkhu</a> (2006). <a class="external text" href="http://www.accesstoinsight.org/tipitaka/mn/mn.062.than.html" rel="nofollow">"Maha-Rahulovada Sutta: The Greater Exhortation to Rahula (MN 62)"</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Maha-Rahulovada+Sutta%3A+The+Greater+Exhortation+to+Rahula+%28MN+62%29&amp;rft.date=2006&amp;rft.au=%E1%B9%ACh%C4%81nissaro+Bhikkhu&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.accesstoinsight.org%2Ftipitaka%2Fmn%2Fmn.062.than.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMeditation"></span> translates this as: "Rahula, develop the meditation [<i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">bhāvana</i></span></i>] of <a href="/wiki/Anapanasati" title="Anapanasati">mindfulness of in-&amp;-out breathing</a>." (Square-bracketed Pali word included based on Ṭhānissaro Bhikkhu, 2006, end note</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-89"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-89">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See, for example, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1" id="CITEREFṬhānissaro_Bhikkhu1997"><a href="/wiki/%E1%B9%ACh%C4%81nissaro_Bhikkhu" title="Ṭhānissaro Bhikkhu">Ṭhānissaro Bhikkhu</a> (1997). <a class="external text" href="http://www.accesstoinsight.org/lib/authors/thanissaro/onetool.html" rel="nofollow">"One Tool Among Many: The Place of Vipassana in Buddhist Practice"</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=One+Tool+Among+Many%3A+The+Place+of+Vipassana+in+Buddhist+Practice&amp;rft.date=1997&amp;rft.au=%E1%B9%ACh%C4%81nissaro+Bhikkhu&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.accesstoinsight.org%2Flib%2Fauthors%2Fthanissaro%2Fonetool.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMeditation"></span>; as well as <a href="#CITEREFKapleau1989">Kapleau (1989</a>, p. 385)<span class="error harv-error" style="display: none; font-size:100%"> harvtxt error: no target: CITEREFKapleau1989 (<a href="/wiki/Category:Harv_and_Sfn_template_errors" title="Category:Harv and Sfn template errors">help</a>)</span> for the derivation of the word "zen" from <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> "dhyāna". <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/P%C4%81li_Text_Society" title="Pāli Text Society">Pāli Text Society</a> Secretary Rupert Gethin, in describing the activities of <a href="/wiki/%C5%9Arama%E1%B9%87a" title="Śramaṇa">wandering ascetics</a> contemporaneous with the Buddha, wrote:
<dl><dd>There is the cultivation of meditative and contemplative techniques aimed at producing what might, for the lack of a suitable technical term in English, be referred to as "altered states of consciousness". In the technical vocabulary of Indian religious texts such states come to be termed "meditations" ([Skt.:] <i>dhyāna</i> / [Pali:] <i>jhāna</i>) or "concentrations" (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sam%C4%81dhi" title="Samādhi">samādhi</a></i>); the attainment of such states of consciousness was generally regarded as bringing the practitioner to deeper knowledge and experience of the nature of the world. (<a href="#CITEREFGethin1998">Gethin 1998</a>, p. 10<span class="error harv-error" style="display: none; font-size:100%"> harvnb error: no target: CITEREFGethin1998 (<a href="/wiki/Category:Harv_and_Sfn_template_errors" title="Category:Harv and Sfn template errors">help</a>)</span>) </dd></dl>
</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-90"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-90">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Examples of contemporary school-specific classics include:
<ul><li>from the Theravada tradition, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFNyanaponika1996">Nyanaponika (Thera) (1996). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=WiPEzgEACAAJ" rel="nofollow"><i>The Heart of Buddhist Meditation: Satipaṭṭhāna: a Handbook of Mental Training Based on the Buddha's Way of Mindfulness, with an Anthology of Relevant Texts Translated from the Pali and Sanskrit</i></a>. Buddhist Publication Society.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Heart+of+Buddhist+Meditation%3A+Satipa%E1%B9%AD%E1%B9%ADh%C4%81na%3A+a+Handbook+of+Mental+Training+Based+on+the+Buddha%27s+Way+of+Mindfulness%2C+with+an+Anthology+of+Relevant+Texts+Translated+from+the+Pali+and+Sanskrit&amp;rft.pub=Buddhist+Publication+Society&amp;rft.date=1996&amp;rft.au=Nyanaponika+%28Thera%29&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DWiPEzgEACAAJ&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMeditation"></span>.</li>
<li>from the Zen tradition, <a href="#CITEREFKapleau1989">Kapleau (1989)</a><span class="error harv-error" style="display: none; font-size:100%"> harvtxt error: no target: CITEREFKapleau1989 (<a href="/wiki/Category:Harv_and_Sfn_template_errors" title="Category:Harv and Sfn template errors">help</a>)</span>. </li></ul>
</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-91"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-91">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFGoldstein2003">Goldstein (2003)</a><span class="error harv-error" style="display: none; font-size:100%"> harvtxt error: no target: CITEREFGoldstein2003 (<a href="/wiki/Category:Harv_and_Sfn_template_errors" title="Category:Harv and Sfn template errors">help</a>)</span> writes that, in regard to the <a href="/wiki/Satipatthana_Sutta" title="Satipatthana Sutta">Satipatthana Sutta</a>, "there are more than fifty different practices outlined in this Sutta. The meditations that derive from these foundations of mindfulness are called <i>vipassana</i>..., and in one form or another – and by whatever name – are found in all the major Buddhist traditions" (p. 92).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-92"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-92">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Regarding Tibetan visualizations, <a href="#CITEREFKamalashila2003">Kamalashila (2003)</a><span class="error harv-error" style="display: none; font-size:100%"> harvtxt error: no target: CITEREFKamalashila2003 (<a href="/wiki/Category:Harv_and_Sfn_template_errors" title="Category:Harv and Sfn template errors">help</a>)</span>, writes: "The Tara meditation ... is one example out of thousands of subjects for visualization meditation, each one arising out of some meditator's visionary experience of enlightened qualities, seen in the form of <a href="/wiki/Buddhahood" title="Buddhahood">Buddhas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bodhisattva" title="Bodhisattva">Bodhisattvas</a>" (p. 227).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-110"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-110">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">These definitions of <i>samatha</i> and <i>vipassana</i> are based on the "Four Kinds of Persons Sutta" (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anguttara_Nikaya" title="Anguttara Nikaya">AN</a> 4.94). This article's text is primarily based on <a href="#CITEREFBodhi2005">Bodhi (2005</a>, pp. 269–70, 440 <i>n</i>. 13)<span class="error harv-error" style="display: none; font-size:100%"> harvtxt error: no target: CITEREFBodhi2005 (<a href="/wiki/Category:Harv_and_Sfn_template_errors" title="Category:Harv and Sfn template errors">help</a>)</span>. See also <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1" id="CITEREFṬhānissaro_Bhikkhu1998d"><a href="/wiki/%E1%B9%ACh%C4%81nissaro_Bhikkhu" title="Ṭhānissaro Bhikkhu">Ṭhānissaro Bhikkhu</a> (1998d). <a class="external text" href="http://www.accesstoinsight.org/tipitaka/an/an04/an04.094.than.html" rel="nofollow">"Samadhi Sutta: Concentration (Tranquillity and Insight) (AN 4.94)"</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Samadhi+Sutta%3A+Concentration+%28Tranquillity+and+Insight%29+%28AN+4.94%29&amp;rft.date=1998&amp;rft.au=%E1%B9%ACh%C4%81nissaro+Bhikkhu&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.accesstoinsight.org%2Ftipitaka%2Fan%2Fan04%2Fan04.094.than.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMeditation"></span>.</span>
</li>
</ol></div>

<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEBondOspinaHootonBialy2009p._135:_&quot;It_is_plausible_that_meditation_is_best_thought_of_as_a_natural_category_of_techniques_best_captured_by_'[[family_resemblance]]s'_([[Ludwig_Wittgenstein|Wittgenstein]],_1968)_or_by_the_related_[[Prototype_(linguistics)|'prototype'_model_of_concepts]]_([[Eleanor_Rosch|Rosch]],_1973;_Rosch_&amp;_Mervin,_1975).&quot;-39"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBondOspinaHootonBialy2009p._135:_&quot;It_is_plausible_that_meditation_is_best_thought_of_as_a_natural_category_of_techniques_best_captured_by_'[[family_resemblance]]s'_([[Ludwig_Wittgenstein|Wittgenstein]],_1968)_or_by_the_related_[[Prototype_(linguistics)|'prototype'_model_of_concepts]]_([[Eleanor_Rosch|Rosch]],_1973;_Rosch_&amp;_Mervin,_1975).&quot;_39-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFBondOspinaHootonBialy2009">Bond et al. 2009</a>, p. 135: "It is plausible that meditation is best thought of as a natural category of techniques best captured by '<a href="/wiki/Family_resemblance" title="Family resemblance">family resemblances</a>' (<a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Wittgenstein" title="Ludwig Wittgenstein">Wittgenstein</a>, 1968) or by the related <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prototype_(linguistics)" title="Prototype (linguistics)">'prototype' model of concepts</a> (<a href="/wiki/Eleanor_Rosch" title="Eleanor Rosch">Rosch</a>, 1973; Rosch &amp; Mervin, 1975).".</span>

<li id="cite_note-41"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-41">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">worldcat.org: Daniel Goleman, <a class="external text" href="https://www.worldcat.org/title/varieties-of-the-meditative-experience/oclc/636007220/editions?editionsView=true" rel="nofollow">The varieties of meditative experience</a>.<i></i></span>

<li id="cite_note-lutz08-43"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-lutz08_43-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFLutzSlagterDunneDavidson2008">Lutz, Antoine; Slagter, Heleen A.; Dunne, John D.; Davidson, Richard J. (April 2008). <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC2693206" rel="nofollow">"Attention regulation and monitoring in meditation"</a>. <i>Trends in Cognitive Sciences</i>. <b>12</b> (4): 163–69. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1016%2Fj.tics.2008.01.005" rel="nofollow">10.1016/j.tics.2008.01.005</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC2693206" rel="nofollow">2693206</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/18329323" rel="nofollow">18329323</a>. <q>The term ‘meditation’ refers to a broad variety of practices...In order to narrow the explanandum to a more tractable scope, this article uses Buddhist contemplative techniques and their clinical secular derivatives as a paradigmatic framework (see e.g., 9,10 or 7,9 for reviews including other types of techniques, such as Yoga and Transcendental Meditation). Among the wide range of practices within the Buddhist tradition, we will further narrow this review to two common styles of meditation, FA and OM (see box 1–box 2), that are often combined, whether in a single session or over the course of practitioner's training. These styles are found with some variation in several meditation traditions, including Zen, Vipassanā and Tibetan Buddhism (e.g. 7,15,16)....The first style, FA meditation, entails voluntary focusing attention on a chosen object in a sustained fashion. The second style, OM meditation, involves non-reactively monitoring the content of experience from moment to moment, primarily as a means to recognize the nature of emotional and cognitive patterns</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Trends+in+Cognitive+Sciences&amp;rft.atitle=Attention+regulation+and+monitoring+in+meditation&amp;rft.volume=12&amp;rft.issue=4&amp;rft.pages=163-69&amp;rft.date=2008-04&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC2693206%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F18329323&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1016%2Fj.tics.2008.01.005&amp;rft.aulast=Lutz&amp;rft.aufirst=Antoine&amp;rft.au=Slagter%2C+Heleen+A.&amp;rft.au=Dunne%2C+John+D.&amp;rft.au=Davidson%2C+Richard+J.&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC2693206&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMeditation"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEBondOspinaHootonBialy2009p._130:_&quot;The_differences_and_similarities_among_these_techniques_is_often_explained_in_the_Western_meditation_literature_in_terms_of_the_direction_of_mental_attention_(Koshikawa_&amp;_Ichii,_1996;_Naranjo,_1971;_Orenstein,_1971):_A_practitioner_can_focus_intensively_on_one_particular_object_(so-called_''concentrative_meditation''),_on_all_mental_events_that_enter_the_field_of_awareness_(so-called_''mindfulness_meditation''),_or_both_specific_focal_points_and_the_field_of_awareness_(Orenstein,_1971).&quot;-44"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBondOspinaHootonBialy2009p._130:_&quot;The_differences_and_similarities_among_these_techniques_is_often_explained_in_the_Western_meditation_literature_in_terms_of_the_direction_of_mental_attention_(Koshikawa_&amp;_Ichii,_1996;_Naranjo,_1971;_Orenstein,_1971):_A_practitioner_can_focus_intensively_on_one_particular_object_(so-called_''concentrative_meditation''),_on_all_mental_events_that_enter_the_field_of_awareness_(so-called_''mindfulness_meditation''),_or_both_specific_focal_points_and_the_field_of_awareness_(Orenstein,_1971).&quot;_44-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFBondOspinaHootonBialy2009">Bond et al. 2009</a>, p. 130: "The differences and similarities among these techniques is often explained in the Western meditation literature in terms of the direction of mental attention (Koshikawa &amp; Ichii, 1996; Naranjo, 1971; Orenstein, 1971): A practitioner can focus intensively on one particular object (so-called <i>concentrative meditation</i>), on all mental events that enter the field of awareness (so-called <i>mindfulness meditation</i>), or both specific focal points and the field of awareness (Orenstein, 1971).".</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-45"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-45">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFEaswaran2018">Easwaran, Eknath (2018). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=bcnJAAAAQBAJ&amp;q=bhagavad+gita" rel="nofollow"><i>The Bhagavad Gita: (Classics of Indian Spirituality)</i></a>. Nilgiri Press. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-58638-019-9" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-58638-019-9"><bdi>978-1-58638-019-9</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Bhagavad+Gita%3A+%28Classics+of+Indian+Spirituality%29&amp;rft.pub=Nilgiri+Press&amp;rft.date=2018&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-58638-019-9&amp;rft.aulast=Easwaran&amp;rft.aufirst=Eknath&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DbcnJAAAAQBAJ%26q%3Dbhagavad%2Bgita&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMeditation"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-46"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-46">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1" id="CITEREFlywa2015">lywa (2 April 2015). <a class="external text" href="https://www.lamayeshe.com/article/developing-single-pointed-concentration" rel="nofollow">"Developing Single-pointed Concentration"</a>. <q>Single-pointed concentration (<a href="/wiki/Samadhi" title="Samadhi">samadhi</a>) is a meditative power that is useful in either of these two types of meditation. However, in order to develop samadhi itself we must cultivate principally concentration meditation. In terms of practice, this means that we must choose an object of concentration and then meditate single-pointedly on it every day until the power of samadhi is attained.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Developing+Single-pointed+Concentration&amp;rft.date=2015-04-02&amp;rft.au=lywa&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.lamayeshe.com%2Farticle%2Fdeveloping-single-pointed-concentration&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMeditation"></span></span>

<ul><li>Focused attention
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Th%C3%ADch_Nh%E1%BA%A5t_H%E1%BA%A1nh" title="Thích Nhất Hạnh">Thich Nhat Hanh</a></li></ul></li>
